RATING: 8
I didn't love Eminent Domain when I first played it. It seemed a little over-complicated, and I didn't really grasp the lead/follow mechanic very well on my first play. With more experience with that mechanic, thanks to [thing=19857][/thing], I tried Eminent Domain again, and had a very different experience. 

The way the core roles fit together is very seamless, and integrating that kind of experience with a deck-builder is sheer genius. Most people will specialize on one or the other role, but you have the option to follow your opponents even if it's a role you have very few of. 

Eminent Domain's multiple paths to victory are nicely considered. I haven't tried Researching my way to victory, but have had a good success with a hybrid colonize/survey role. I'd also be curious to see how the military strategy could work -- could be a nice way to zig when others zag.

Overall, Eminent Domain carries a really pleasant decision space within a short play-time. Tableau-building is one of my favorite mechanics, and Eminent Domain mixes it up with a solid deck-builder, that doesn't overstay its welcome and provides a fun challenge. Every time I play the game it sticks with me for days to come, so I'm really looking forward to playing this one more.

First impressions after three games: 

A solid 7. It's clearly a well-designed and (almost over-)produced game that looks really nice and I kinda like it. It's no [i]Race for the Galaxy[/i], but a good card game nevertheless. I'm glad I backed it. 

I find it a bit dull and abstract, though, and there's hardly any theme (I mean, sure there is a theme, you just don't really feel it at all while playing). Can't really put my finger on what's missing, but somehow I feel something is.. Definitely need to play much more to really grok the game.

Too bad not too many people seem interested in playing it. My gf hates it (but then again, that was to be expected as she hates most games..).

RATING: 6
This game is niether a ironic attempt at making a Hitchikers Guide to the Galaxy game or a game about a intergalactic government forcing planets to sell their land so that it can build walmarts on them. It is a medium weight galactic civ game that incorporates a deck building mechanic to move the game along and whose only sins are that it incorporates a very popular mechanic I never really liked too much and that it is a sort of "middle child" of galactic civ builders in my family of board games. The mechanic I am referring to is the ability to follow suit when someone plays a like card like you would see in Glory to Rome and Puerto Rico. The "middle child" thing is that every time I wanted to get this game to the table to try it again and give it a fair shake, my attention went to other games about galactic civ building like Among the Stars, Core Worlds, and Roll for the Galaxy. Perhaps the expansion has made it better or I should give it another try. If someone asks me to play it before I get an offer to trade it for something, I wouldn't be opposed to the idea.

RATING: 3.5
Indeed a much better refined deck building game than Dominion ever was or will be.

With limited choices and deviation with technology cards, snatching VPs even on your opponents turn is a big plus. Does away with the solitaire part of such deck building games.

The production and trade mechanic brings a good add on to the theme and produces a logical VP that links well to the game system and theme.

Nevertheless, it still falls victim to repeated plays on such deck building games.....boredom sets in as eventually gamers will see it as nothing more than an exercise on 'shuffling'.

Good effort wasted on a lousy mechanic.

RATING: 7
Take a deck building game and make it one mechanic in a different game and that's what you get here. The deck building is one aspect of the game. Nice idea and nicely done in this game. The turns get a little samey after a while though. I like the level of player interaction in the game. That's one thing that I haven't liked about some of the deck building games that are out. Too much interaction though, as in Nightfall, is overkill. I think the designer got the balance just right here. All of the strategies that I have tried seem pretty balanced and the games I've played have been tense and enjoyable. Overall a solid game with no negative aspects but the games I've played have seemed a bit dull. It's a well thought out game that is fun but overall I don't think there is anything super special about the game. I doubt it'll be breaking into many players top 10 lists any time soon but it's a good 8 in my book. However, I can see this game getting downgraded in a couple of years as designers take the deck building idea new places in future games. Sure it was one of the first to do something different with deck building but I doubt anyone will remember that in 3-5 years unfortunately.

RATING: 4
A slower drafting game, Eminent Domain tends to have very satisfying individual turns. As you settle or conquer planets, your economy grows, allowing other tasks to become easier which in turn makes other tasks easier, all the while you're trying to slim your deck down so that it doesn't bog with useless duplicates. Unfortunately, the "slimming" part falls flat since removing cards from one's hand is too costly and usually never has enough thinning available. The result is that the midgame to endgame is just bogged for every player, even ones who are thinning like mad. It's a bit overproduced and certainly those ugly Buck Rogers ships that you see in every space game are unwelcome. Ultimately, while some of the middle of the game is fun, the end game payoff is almost always a wet noodle. You do all this work and it comes down to point salad at the end. Also, unlike Dominion getting one extra action per turn is completely broken. I recommend not allowing players to take "Productivity" as rushing to 7 research for it is the strongest and easiest guaranteed win strategy in the game. The game takes over an hour to play even when not trying to be competitive, so the payoffs are rarely worth it for a game about as in-depth as faster deckbuilders such as Star Realms or Dominion.

Update: Demoted to a 4. This game never sees play anymore because of the obvious and irreparable flaws. Some of the strategies being outright broken and unstoppable combined with the insanely long play time make it a lot less appealing than other deckbuilders out there. It's way too competitive a genre to tolerate this nonsense.

RATING: 6
Purchased Eminent Domain with both expansions in the delivery package. One play of vanilla Eminent Domain under my belt, and -- all in all -- Jen and I found it a nice, albeit light, diversion. 

+ The game play was fun. 

+ The techs and planets seem fun, in concept. 

However, if we didn't have Escalation and Exotica, I'd probably find it a touch tame, and lacking in variety. The core/base experience is certainly fun, and would be better than scrabble for passing the time whilst trapped someplace (cruise ship? I don't know, never been on one), but I probably wouldn't seek it out unless my options were really limited. 

That said, I highly suspect escalation will add the needed depth to put it over the top. 

Only major minus, thus far, is the game is a touch long for what it is (and I feel the same about Dominion). I found myself making strategically suboptimal decisions just to drive down the clock (and cheated to close the game out by taking two cards to empty the game closing stack--a move which spoke to my desire to see things end rather than a wish for competitive gain). 

Worth adding I purchased this till fill a card game void, and I think it works nicely. A few more plays will help me decide if the experience is just too long for what it offers (and, if that's the case, I'll experiment with removing cards from the stacks to find a sweet spot). 

Humm.. Not sure about the rating here. Right now? 6/10 seems about right. 

I suspect that will go up.

RATING: 6
I'm a great fan of Race for the Galaxy and Eminent Domain puts us in a very similar place in space.  Here again are planets, technological enhancements, military options, production and trade.  Yet it seems a bit emptier in Eminent Domain's part of the universe.  There's little of the clutter of icons that is found in RftG.  That's in some ways a good thing as it shortens ED's learning curve; and yet it makes me wonder about replay value.  While ED offers alternative strategies, I'd expect next game's colonization strategy to play out pretty much the same as this one's.  For now, I'm quite enjoying ED's blend of mechanics pilfered from RftG, Dominion and Glory to Rome, although I fear it may not last.

ED's artwork isn't memorable, but at least it stays out of the way of the game.  Production quality is decent.  We struggled a little with the rules.

Update:  another play and, as I feared, ED has shown me nothing new.  Its merits remain that it is easy to learn and a well-honed design, with no rough edges.  However, I don't expect to play it again unless others request.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Includes:
[thing=136313][/thing]

Sci-fi Euro with role selection and lots of different paths to victory.  This caught my attention a while ago, and since I've played it several times I've learned to like it very quickly.  The different powers of playing cards during the Action or Role phases really changes how I've previously viewed hand management.  That said, the base game has a lot of sameness to it, and while you can end up trashing lots of cards quickly through Research actions, I find that the out-of-the-box experience is a little bland.

However, adding Escalation to Eminent Domain is incredible!  I think the base game is still fine without this expansion (it's not a game whose first expansion is "the other half of the base game").  That said, what Seth Jaffee added to Eminent Domain through Escalation takes the game to a whole other level.  Asymmetric starts through Scenarios are awesome, and all very balanced through tons of playtesting.  The level of interaction between different classes of ships as a resource for conquest and technology is very high and intuitive, the new technologies are awesome (looking at you, Double Time), and the tighter and clearer paths to victory really help streamline the game.  Again, the base game is still a good experience, but I highly recommend anyone interested in Eminent Domain to pick up Escalation as well!

My primary critique is game length. Every single time I play I feel that the game is too short--it ends too quickly. You necessarily run out the role piles faster than I'd like. My secondary critique is one of mechanics. Put simply, I'd rather play Roll for the Galaxy or Puerto Rio if I want good role-selection. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This is not a deck building game! Well... it almost is, but I'd rather place it in a category of its own. When people say deck building game, my thought runt to Dominion or games alike, but Eminent Domain is much more than that. The strategy is more complex, the game play is much longer than what is written on the box (-1 point) and the aim of the game is not to add as many cards as you can in your deck but rather have the right cards in the deck at the right moment.

Eminent Domain is an innovative game, it plays well in two - which is not typical for games that are not especially designed for two - and ... well, I want to play it again

RATING: 6.5
This rating is after a few plays of the base game. The base game screams out for incorporation of the expansions and bonus planets for replayability/more interesting plays. However, the mechanisms seem to be a good mix of deck-building, tableau building and a GtR-style follow interaction.

Edit, June 2017: I like this game quite a bit. It just fits in a weird space where other games get pulled out more often. I find that it's a little more opaque to new players than other more typical deck builders. I also find it a bit less accessible to new players than Roll/Race for the Galaxy. Roll/Race's accessibility is dependent, primarily, on understanding iconography. This game requires players to be comfortable with both some iconography/card abilities and some atypical game mechanisms. This game likely needs a dedicated group willing to stick with it for a long time. I want to give this game a 7-8, but, sadly, I can't due how much it gets played. The idea of it really appeals to me and the interesting mix of mechanisms will allow it stay in my collection for a long time.

Extras/Promos/Expansions: 
Eminent Domain: Bonus Planets (2011)
Eminent Domain: Escalation (2014)
Eminent Domain: Exotica (2016)

RATING: 8
Played a few play-tests.

After playing Race for the Galaxy, Dominion, and Glory to Rome; I find that I like Eminent Domain more than Race for the Galaxy and Glory to Rome.  The deckbuilding aspect is their as a mechanic and not the whole game so it is actually quite different from Dominion.  Race for the Galaxy and Glory to Rome are both very dependent on what cards become available and rather you can get your strategy going based on that.  Eminent Domain allows you to build your strategy and manipulate it over the course of the game.  I see a great advantage to this.  It's also fairly simply so teaching to new players should be easy.

I can't wait to get a copy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
Kickstarter Limited Edition FINALLY arrived. Box #15/500 Played a few times tonight, not sure I'm 'getting' it yet...

Some notes:

- I like the way that following has a variable relationship to leading. Leading the same thing too often ends the game really fast and pads out your deck with the wrong cards. On the other hand, if you ca leech off another player for warfare or colonise, you get full utility out of your deck without draining the stacks. Learning to manage that seems like half the battle.

- The game feels partly (and oddly!) unfinished - there are four types of resource but this only has an effect when a given technology comes into play. There are three types of fighter token, and they feature int he game art (which suggests they knew beforehand that these components would be int he game), yet there is no rules distinction drawn between the three tokens.

- The cards are very crappy quality. They are thin and flimsy. I didn't sleeve my RftG cards, and after over a hundred plays they are looking very used, but mainly around the edges and they look more worn than banged up. After only three plays of ED the cards were starting to show wear, and that bad kind of wear where the printed face peels back a little bit from the cardstock, leaving chunks of white showing through.

- Personally, I don't like the art. It's too cartoony. I'm warming up to the graphic design of the cards, which at first I thought was too colourful for a space themed game.

- On a related note, selling off the option to name cards during the kickstarter process has made for some VERY strange planet names. Some are called totally outlandish things like  'Warlord Betzel's Domain' (or whatever), while others are names in reverse (like 'Sednim', Mindes backwards), while still others are just people's names ('Evan Platt'). It makes for a very incoherent set of names and bugs me in a small way.

- Benj pointed out that warfare and colonise work very unthematically, and the name of each role should be reversed (while the mechanic remains identical) - Thematically, your fleet would be tied up on a planet and would come back to your deck all at once (the way colonise works), while colonists would be slowly built up and then would permanently be assigned to a planet, reducing your pool (the way warfare currently works). It's not a problem, just an interesting observation.

- While I haven't yet plumbed the depths of complexity available through the tech tree, I can't help but feel the tech tree is too limited. I really want twice the number and variety of tech cards.

- Similarly, I don't think the game scales well because you only get 16/20 of each role card. 4 players is quite short, 2 players can drag on. More cards and variable stacks would seem to make more sense.

- Some strategies (trade/produce, I'm looking at you) seem REALLY hard to manage without draining the deck super fast. I'm coming from RftG, where produce/consume can go on forever and the other players have to race to finish the game to stop you winning, while military is a fast 'rush' strategy, in EmDo it's almost exactly the opposite.

- Despite my two comparisons (one in terms of card quality and the other in terms of strategy), comparisons to RftG are vastly overstated, as are comparisons to Dominion. The only thing EmDo shares with RftG is a few similarities - the broad theme, the word 'settle' and the dual produce/trade role. Role selection in EmDo seems more inspired by Glory to Rome, but comparisons there are overstated too. In Rftg, almost every card is unique, and you need to carefully slot it into a strategy. In GtR there are three copies of each card, but each has a specific effect. in EmDo there are basic generic role cards that form most of your deck, plus research cards that let you make tiny tweaks to the deck.

- That's not to say EmDo isn't derivative - it really is. The only mechanic I personally have never seen before is the learning deck - as you do actions your deck gets better at them. The inclusion of that is extremely clever, and makes the game pretty different to everything else in the Deckbuilding category.

Got rid of pre-Melbourne move - gave to Damon

RATING: 6
It has role selection very much like Glory to Rome.  It has planet colonize / conquer / produce / trade like Race for the Galaxy.  It is slightly "deck-building" like Dominion / Thunderstone / etc etc etc. (but really it feels more like "deck-clogging" than "building" with all of the role cards you have to take.)

So, it has aspects of several games that I like, but I don't think it uses any of these aspects as well as their originators.  They just feel forced together, and it doesn't seem to jive well.

I appreciate that there are very few unique cards to learn and the mechanics are very straightforward, but in the end it just isn't a great game.

What's up with the title for this one? I don't understand what it's supposed to mean in this context. Kim wandered by and wondered if we were sleazy property developers getting local governments to expropriate property from old people and sell it to us to make a huge profit. It's a very strange title.
---
This is a clever mash-up of other people's ideas, and it does play reasonably cleanly and smoothly, but my strong sucpicion is that there isn't much going on here. The reinforcement of actions - you have to add a card to your deck for an action each time you take it - is the sole identifiable piece of originality, and it seems somewhat problematic - because it makes it so comparatively expensive to do anything you don't specialize in, and comparatively hard to change course, you get locked in to a course of action pretty early. Anyway, I don't want to judge it too harshly, because it's good for a few games, but I suspect the replay value will turn out to be low.
---
No rating yet, but first play was reasonably positive, even though I doubt the game has much staying power. It's completely unoriginal, being basically a mash-up of Race for the Galaxy and Glory to Rome, but it's pretty streamlined and compact. It doesn't quite feel right, the small hand size, often limited benefits for following, and the somewhat slow and uninteresting pace of colonizing and conquering leaves me feeling like it isn't quite balanced right, but there are interesting and different tensions in the deck management element. Worth trying out if you like the various games being ripped off.

RATING: 5
[i]2 plays.
4 players.

[/i]This is yet another game that has been out for a little while, but which LobsterTrap gave me a chance to take my first real look at. The first play went rather poorly for me, as I struggled to learn tech trees at the core of the game. Additionally, I forgot the endgame condition, leading me too over-invest in game strategies premised on a longer session. Because of this, I made an effort to play again before the end of the con just so that I could have an opportunity to play better.  In my second game, things ran more smoothly and I very nearly won. 

After two games, I am still not convinced that I would choose this game over [thing=28143]Race for the Galaxy[/thing]. However, it is an enjoyable game. If you are not already a Race fanatic, I can recommend exploring this as an alternative option. The games are not identical (and I hope that I am not implying that they are full substitutes). But, as mentioned above, I am not a fan of tableau games. And I do not see the need to own many games that share similar qualities. 

Some people have suggested that [thing=68425][/thing] is more akin to [thing=36218]Dominion[/thing]. I see where they are coming from, but I felt that it was much too tactical to support such comparisons. [thing=36218]Dominion[/thing] is a game that requires long-term strategy, chosen at the outset. [thing=68425][/thing], by contrast, seems to turn on see ability to manage one's hand, and to acquire technologies that allow for more efficient use of one's current cards. In this sense, it is more reminiscent of [thing=28143][/thing]. This is a decent game that I would likely play anytime it was offered, assuming there are no better options on the table. Mostly, this is due to the short duration of the game; 30 minutes or so with four players seems just about right and inoffensive enough that I wouldn't bother to push back against it at a game day. But neither am I enthralled with the game, so I think it ends up at roughly a [BGCOLOR=#9999FF][b][COLOR=#9999FF]_[/COLOR]6[COLOR=#9999FF]_[/COLOR][/b][/BGCOLOR].

RATING: 5
I am just not a fan of deck building in general. There is too much exactitude for me. Remembering how many of each card you have, and what is remaining in your deck, planning out sequences so the deck is giving you what you need when you need it. 

You would never be able to describe a move in a game like this as swashbuckling. There are no "oohs" and "ahhs" over the table. There is zero decision tension. 

And I can see this being harmless for a few plays, but I fail to see why games would be substantially different from one another after a few plays.

Meh! It’s a little mix of GtR, RftG, Dominion and such games, but it’s much worse than any of those… It really wasn’t engaging to play. Maybe it was because we played it with 4 with 3 new players, but it really didn’t click for any of the new players. Won’t refuse to play it (with 2 or 3), but won’t pursue it either.

RATING: 4
A decent, albeit ultimately forgettable game. Mashes up deckbuilding, tableau building and role-selection in a somewhat interesting way, but the ultimate effect is watery and domestic, without the stimulating variety and unexpected card-synergies of Race For the Galaxy, or the strategic depth of Dominion. It is probably the best of the crop of post-Dominion deckbuilders I've tried (at least as far as pure card games are concerned), but that's really not saying much considering how much derivative shite followed in the wake of that classic. I'd play it again if there were nothing better on offer, but it would take a pretty grim set of alternatives.

RATING: 9
Bought this game as a 'Race for the Galaxy' lite. But that is being unfair to this game and RFTG. While they share a very similar theme, they are uniquely different, and have different play styles. The biggest positive for this game is the simplicity of it's play design, and the ease of teaching it to new comers. These two features alone means it gets to the table more often than many other games I own.

If I have one complaint about the game, it is the quality of the cards - while they are gorgeous, they just don't feel very sturdy. This has been resolved by placing them all in sleeves, which actually works quite well.

RATING: 7
Deck and tableau building card game.  Play goes in rounds.  A player first chooses and performs an action, or may skip that.  The player then must choose a role.  Other players may play cards of that role to follow or may draw a card from their deck instead.  In choosing a role, a role card is taken from the board and put into the player's deck.  This may be augmented with additional cards from hand.

The object is to add worlds to one's tableau by either settling or conquering them.  Most worlds are worth victory points, may generate extra symbols to augment roles, or may produce goods.  These goods can be sold for victory points.  Having enough worlds of a type allow one to research technology cards.  These cards, different for each of the three world types, provide better actions.  At the second and third levels, they also provide victory points.

Combining technology that aids in better production and consumption can be very powerful.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
Components: Nice artwork on the planet and research cards. Good iconography. Very cool ships. The design of the role cards, though, which occupy the bulk of the game activity, is very plain.

Experience: Dominion + Race for the Galaxy, but without being as good as either. The overlap is so extensive that I don't see this game adding much to my gaming life. (It's a bit of a turn off for me how the roles map so similarly to RftG phases, how the starting deck size and hand size match those in Dominion, and how there are the same number of VP chips as in a 2-player game of RftG; just feels like a mash-up.) 

It's cool how cards can be used as actions, as roles, or to follow another player. But it gets really confusing when someone chooses their action, and we all think we can follow it, since we're using the same language to describe actions and roles.

Military seems much more powerful than colonizing, since cards don't get tied up on the table (and can thus continue to produce ships), and I needn't commit my ships to any particular planet until I have enough to conquer. I follow on colonize just to get the cards out of my deck for free.

Like in RftG, I can see there are some different strategies to employ. I want some diversity, because that will allow me to be flexible and piggy-back off my opponents, but mostly this seems to be a game of specialization, to pick a couple of roles that work well together and hammer them into the ground.

The game is over very quickly, but without being satisfying. Lives in the shadow of its predecessors.

First Played: 2011-10-11
Last Played: 2012-03-06
Eagerness to Play Again: No Desire

Plays (4):
4 players: 4

RATING: 9

Thankfully, it's also really really good. :)

Unlike many a deck builder where you sit patiently awaiting your turn, thus game allows you to take actions on other players turns.

The planets are the main source of points (though you can get into producing and trading resources as well) and you can peacefully colonise them or cry havoc and go military!

It's a great game and the only reason I'm not giving it a 10 as it gets better with the expansions, so 9 it is.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Eminent Domain, by Seth Jafee, is a meaty card game that shares some lineage from Dominon, Race For the Galaxy, and, most noticeably, Glory to Rome. I went to BGG.Con with the expectation that I would play it, like it, and pre-order it through the Kickstarter program. That did not happen.

Eminent Domain is centered around role cards, each of which serves both as a tool for performing an action as well as a means to lead with a role or follow someone else’s role. These cards generally serve to allow a player to add planets from the draw deck into the play area in front of them, claim these planets to get their benefits, produce and harvest resources for victory points, or research new technologies that give special action powers and cards that are available for multiple roles.

On each turn, a player optionally plays a card for an action, leads in a role, thus adding a card of that role to the deck, discards any cards from their hand, and draws new cards until they hit their hand limit. This repeats itself over and over again until the game comes to a completion by either depleting the victory point pool or until a number of decks run out based on the number of players.

I found my plays of Eminent Domain to be enjoyable, but a bit repetitive. I am sure that there are all sorts of interesting strategies to be found in the game, but the lack of character in the cards, particularly in comparison to the games which it is similar to, held me back from really liking the game. With all of the other meaty card games that I own and like (7 Wonders, Innovation, Glory to Rome, Race For the Galaxy), and the amount of time I currently spend on these sorts of card games (not much), it just doesn’t belong in my collection.

[blogpost=6852][/blogpost]
[blogpost=4742][/blogpost]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Players are space travelling races, surveying and settling planets via colonisation or conquest.  A fast moving card game, where players on their turn play a card for an optional action, then play another card (which can be boosted with more like cards) for a role.  Actions and roles do stuff like survey planets, increase technology, conquer or colonise planets, and so on.  All this stuff is done through card play and deck building.  The idea is to get the most influence (i.e. victory) points through planet acquisition, trade and technology purchases.  This game borrows a lot of stuff from the recently heavyweights such as Glory To Rome, Dominion and the like.  To it's credit, it does feel different enough from the others, but the similarities sure are there.  It all leads back to the old gamer's dilemma of why play this when you can play Glory To Rome?  Perhaps if space is your thing...

RATING: 9
Deckbuilding games come and go, but very few are elegant enough to not need lots of expansions to keep people interested.  Eminent Domain is one of those few.  The mechanics are simple, and yet the game play is deep and rewarding.  Although at first glance it seems fairly straightforward and one-dimensional, there is so much subtlety to how to play any given hand of cards that skill will almost invariably win out over luck, and to me that's the hallmark of a great game.  This is, to my mind, the best and most elegantly designed deckbuilder to date.

RATING: 6
I got to hand it to TMG - they make pretty games.

Eminent Domain is an interesting deckbuilder.  When you hear or see that word, chances are you think of Dominion-style games where you buy cards from a pool.  Most of the time you end up thinking, "Huh, I'd rather just play Dominion," or even, "Eh, I'm sick of deckbuilding entirely."  Fair enough, but take a look at this one.  It might not grab you by the yayas and pull you into its world - I think we're all past that point now, but ED has a neat take on the genre (at least I've not seen it before).

While you still build your deck by drawing from a common pool, the neat thing here is that you're not playing cards for currency and buying stuff up.  The common pool is simply all the types of actions that you can do, and that's it.  If you have some in your hand, you can play them for an action, but when you draw from the pool, you are basically doing action selection and then boosting that same action with similar cards from your hand.  Then, should a player wish to do so, they can follow your action out of turn at the cost of staring their own turn with a smaller hand, which typically isn't going to hurt too much since your hand probably sucks if you're going to follow.  Of course, there are benefits for leading the action on your turn, but otherwise it gives you an opportunity to get some stuff done before you get some stuff done... if that makes sense.

I liked it.  I don't know if it's something that I would play regularly, but I liked its twist of action-selection  blended with deckbuilding.  The unique cards are your research cards and planets you can "draft" into your tableau, but you'll have to settle them first before they can become useful to you (generally by providing icons; think free money here).

That being said, despite its unique outlook on the genre and its overall beautiful graphic design, I felt a bit of a lack of engagement.  I don't expect a lot of theme from a deckbuilder, but even still, I need a little bit of it to stay engaged.  Core Worlds, in contrast, is insanely meaty and feels like you're building an empire.  The Legendary series also play into their themes quite well.  Eminent Domain... felt like gaming cards and never took me out of the meta.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Let's look at aspects of the following:

Dominion: Deck building/thinning

Glory to Rome: Choosing roles and other players being able to follow that role or draw cards.  As well as boosting role actions.

Race for the Galaxy: Similar sci-fi theme and actions such as settling colonies, conquering worlds, and being able to produce goods and exchange them for VP

Eminent Domain is a combination of all of these games but yet feels like it's own unique thing.  On the complexity side, it's much closer to Dominion than Glory to Rome or Race for the Galaxy.  This sometimes leads to the game feeling fun to some or being considered tame/boring to others depending on where your tastes lie.  I have heard friends call London a relaxing game and I understand the sentiment but it feels too long to be relaxing.  I find Eminent Domain to be relaxing and relatively quick.  In fact, players should be wary that if multiple people are trying to use similar roles in the game the game will end very quickly almost to the point of being unsatisfying.  Overall, it's enjoyable and I will probably use it as a gateway to the more complex card games in my collection that I prefer.  I'm hoping the game holds up to repeated plays (at least long enough for an expansion to be released).

RATING: 6.2
I've played this a couple dozen times, mostly two player, and am enjoying it a lot.  The follow/dissent mechanism works really well, and adds that bit of interaction that makes this way better than Dominion.  It still doesn't have quite as much interaction as I was hoping, but not too bad.

The game really works quite well; the different elements (flipping planets, researching, and selling goods for points) all integrate nicely, and provide a good amount of tension.  I really like the fact that thinning your deck (via the research action) is always an option.  It really scratches the deck-management itch.

Play time for us is a a little longer than reported, but it is worth it.

Overall, I like this game, and yet it doesn't quite have the touch of greatness it would need to become an 8.  Like most card games, it is short on elegance, and probably doesn't have huge depth (although we are still exploring that.  So far we are not tired of it, but I think that the luster will eventually fade.  It also does have a bit more luck than I like, particularly in the planet deck.  Also, as a minor irk, there are a few bits that seem "incomplete," as if it is just waiting for the next expansion.  I'm not a big expansion fan; I like complete games.  

But these are all minor points.  It's a good game.  7.5

2-8-2014  Not playing this quite as much anymore. The problem is that the significant luck factor in the planet draw makes me feel that it is a fairly light game, which I want to go by quickly, but the fact that there is a good amount to think about makes my wife feel that it is a serious strategy game that she should take her time with, and so drags it out a little too long for me.  But 

I still like the deck manipulation quite well

4-27-2016 We haven't played this for a couple of years, and I can't really see myself going back to it - it doesn't hold any interest. It was fun enough for a while, but apparently doesn't have lasting charm. Down to a 6.2 (cause it's still better than Dominion)

RATING: N/A
The bottom line: "Eminent Domain is certainly indebted to its respected ancestors, such as Dominion, Race for the Galaxy, and Glory to Rome, and unmistakably bears their influence in its design, in terms of the deck-building and role-selection mechanics, and also the space theme that it shares with Race for the Galaxy. But this blend of mechanics and influences does result in a product that is quite different from anything we've seen before, and allows it to stand on its own despite being constructed on a foundation that is largely derivative. If you enjoy these other titles, there's a very good chance you'll love the card-play and dynamics of Eminent Domain enough for it to earn a place in your collection on the basis of its own merits."

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
New Years day.  This is my first new game played in 2012.  

Another deck building game, but another good one.  I guess I am not tired of them yet.  

This is one of the easier to play deck building games.  Even though it is not complex, the combination of familiar mechanics creates a new strategy platform.  This game is not interactive, yet paying sharp attention to how an opponent is playing is vitally important.  

The interesting mechanic that makes this game unique, is that the more you do a particular role, the better it becomes.  However, at some point it can choke your hand, and also end the game very fast.  Like other deck building games, thinning the deck can be a key to winning.

There are strong similarities to the Client mechanic in Glory to Rome.  If your hand does get choked with one type of card, many actions that you could share with your opponents are lost.  Like Race for the Galaxy, the planets on your tableau can produce one resource which can be exchanged for V.P.'s.  Like Dominion, there are some stacks of cards which are all the same and when a certain number is gone, signals the end of the game.  And the start deck is the same for everyone, as it is with many of the deck building games.

Components:  The plastic fighters are great looking, but there seems to be no reason for the very different sizes and shapes.  Will this be addressed in some expansion?  The cards are of very poor quality--before the first game finished some of the corners showed wear--I sleeved immediately.  In contrast, the start planets are thick cardboard with a nice finish.  I'm not sure why they are so different from the rest of the cards.  The game is visually attractive.  The rules are nicely written, but a clear explanation of how the symbols on the tech cards are used is sadly lacking.

Despite my displeasure with the horrible card quality, and my lack of understanding some of the components' design logic, I really enjoy the play.  I am looking forward to testing different strategies.

RATING: 8.8
Ordered via Kickstarter.  I really like this game.  Its like a lighter, easier to understand Race for the Galaxy in terms of theme and superficial structure/mechanics.  But it is really its own semi-deck builder that is difficult to master and that rewards repeated play.  The two-player game is outstanding.  The three player extended game is very good.

But... the four player game is simply too short.  The game ends just as we are getting our respective "victory engines" cranked up.

I know the experts tell us that this is simply because we are not playing optimally (in other words too many of us are grabbing all the research/colonize cards and not enough of us are doing the warfare approach), but that isn't what makes a game great.  It shouldn't require four experts to play perfectly for it to shine.   That said, I'd play any time whether with 2, 3 or 4.  I can't wait for the expansion.

Very fun game that is much easier to set up and play than other deck-builders, yet remains tough to master.  I love it with 2 players and 3 players and like it with 4.

RATING: 6
When playing Eminent Domain for the first time, comparisons to Race for the Galaxy, Dominion, and Glory to Rome are inevitable. In fact, you can mostly explain the game in terms of those three, although it does simplify the core concepts of each.

Maybe it's because of this that I would brand the game as a beginners' RftG or GtR (Dominion not so much, since the deck-building aspect is much less prominent), introducing many of the core concepts but in a more forgiving environment. The number of available options is fairly small, the combo building part is rather tame, and the action leeching is much less severe without becoming unimportant.

All this simplification comes for a price, of course. The cards, including actions, roles, developments, and planets, are all very generic and provide very little in the way of flavour or character. While I wouldn't go so far as to say the game has no soul, it does smell of clean-room engineering very strongly. I'm also somewhat unsure of the long-term appeal since there is so little variability. I'm sure you will be able to further refine strategies for a while but I expect those to be very small incremental steps without much of a feeling of discovery or improvement.

RATING: 7
Own: Bonus Planets (present in copy I purchased)


This is a very fun, next-gen deckbuilder where your deck more reflects your amount of specialization rather than just action cards and stuff like that. 

It's very much like if you were playing San Juan, but every time you were Builder you got a little bit better at being the Builder.

Plus the dual nature of the cards (Action/Role) is very clever; the interaction comes with "Following" an opponent's roles and playing your role selection in a way that he can't or won't benefit.

Though what makes the game are the cool Research/tech cards.  Those are fun to use, especially when you ramp up your tech curve.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[i]Super Awesome 2014 Birthday Gift from Jen!![/i]

Eminent Domain fortunately lived up to my expectations: a very solid deck builder that does something different. Dominion is all about a variety of cards that come into the hand while Thunderstone just takes Dominion to the next level. ED flips the mechanic of its head. Players now work to specialize in action/role cards in order to maximize particular choices. Of course, balancing these cards is key; but also important is when to begin specializing in one or two types. As well, players must keep in mind how their turns might help out their opponents through the "follow" mechanics (an excellent and defining part of this game). Very streamlined rules and and awesome components. Each planet or technology offers just a couple of extra things to make the game rich without feeling bloated. I can only imagine this gets better with the expansion. When it comes to deck builders, ED might be the classiest of them all. I still prefer Dominon for the sheer variety and Thunderstone for the powerful theme and perfect saturation of mechanics; but ED is definitely on my list of smart deck builders that I'm happy to have in my collection.

All games played now include the 'Escalation' and 'Bonus Planets' expansions.

RATING: 6
Eminent Domain is a little bit better than I expected it to be, but ultimately it doesn't measure up to its competitors. It's a very close relative of some outstanding games like Race for the Galaxy, Glory to Rome, and Dominion. I did enjoy my first play of Eminent Domain, but I can't imagine a situation in which I would choose to play it over those other games. It's just too generic, too flavorless, too patterned, and too limited in scope.

The most interesting part of Eminent Domain is the way that a new "basic" card enters your deck every turn, matching the role that you choose. You have a lot of control over what cards you will be drawing in this game, and your deck cycles very quickly. Managing your deck composition, so that you're drawing the hands you need, is very enjoyable. You have to know what's in your deck. Even one card more of less of a certain type can make a gigantic difference.

I would play Eminent Domain again if asked, but would not request it.

RATING: 9
This game is really growing on me.

RATING: 9
Great deck building space exploration/exploitation card game.  Scratches the same itch as Race for the Galaxy without making me feel I need a math degree to win.  After a few plays, I'm starting to see that there may be a metagame way to win every time, just like in Dominion, but if you stay away from that type of player and keep it light, it is quite fun.  It's also cool to see "emptying a stack to end the game" as having more strategy depth than just as a timer for the game.  Would like to see rules for using warfare to attack other players and we may make our own house rule to allow for that as warfare seems a weaker overall strategy than colonizing, especially since you get one less Warfare card to begin with.  Game is fun without feeling the need to "thin" your deck all the time as the starting cards are as powerful as all but a few cards that you will add to your starting deck.  The addition of a "Political" card that allows you to trade it in for anything else allows you to pick a particular strategy to give an early boost to. Kinda disappointed to hear of a sequel as I don't see how it will help the game and might unnecessarily complicate the game (I'm looking at you 7 Wonders Leaders!).  Could use a way to attack other players though to make Warfare a more viable part of the game, thought.  Highly recommend to any light gamers, especially those that enjoy other deckbuilders.

RATING: 6.5
So, do I enjoy the game?  Yes … but I am not enthralled.  It is fun and has some challenges, but it is not terribly deep.  There are more choices to be made and greater challenges in all of the three games that seem to have influenced its design:  Dominion, Puerto Rico and Race with the Galaxy.  It is difficult not to compare a game to other games, particularly if they share similar characteristics and/or mechanisms.  If I had never played those three games I might be enamored by Eminent Domain.  However, I have played all three of those games, and prefer two of the three.  

Still, Eminent Domain is entertaining, light game that can be played quickly.  Further, since it is not complicated, it is suitable as a vehicle to introduce folks to our hobby.  There is no direct interaction or aggression.  Each player can play their own game without interference from their opponents.  These are all traits that help make it appealing to folks new to the hobby or folks who do not enjoy player conflict.  Reaction from others will likely be mixed.

RATING: 5
This was a solid game but I was a little disappointed. There is less interaction than base set Dominion.  Yes, you read that correctly.  ED is basically a hodge podge of mechanics, and plays a little bit like dominion with glory to rome's roles grafted on.  (I haven't played Race for the galaxy but others compared it to that game too).

Much of the variability and interesting stuff in the game comes from the technology cards, so if you don't start building technology early you can still win but the game is not nearly as interesting.  I built a strong trade economy and was doing well with military conquests of planets but almost completely ignored technology.  I did not win, but did pretty well.  Downside was, I barely used all the neat variable technology cards.  The upgrade cards would be a lot more interesting if technology was not the only possible way to get them.  

The only interaction in the game comes from piggy-backing on other people's role choices.  Sometimes you didn't take a role because you knew no one had taken it in a while and much of the table would benefit.  Other times you intentionally took a role because you knew someone had just done it and few would benefit.  For me, this was not enough interaction.  This game could very easily be converted into a solo game, and other than a few instances where role selection was effected by what had happened before, it was a solo game. 

The game also feels very repetitive.  You do the exact same thing every turn, and your best play is usually dictated by the cards in your hand.  Much like dominion, the best play each turn is brainless and obvious, based solely on what cards you drew- that part of the game almost plays itself.  The real strategy in ED (like dominion) is in what stuff you get to add to your deck and build your chances of drawing a killer hand.  This is not my favorite type of game, but clearly with the popularity of dominion and many clones, I am in the minority.  I enjoy these sort of games but will never clamor to play them because at the end of the day it just isn't enough interesting choices for the time it takes to play.  

The art, cards, and everything else look great.  Game just isn't my cup of tea.  I wouldn't mind playing it a second time to make sure I'm not selling it short.

RATING: 7
This game contains elements of Race for the Galaxy (pick one of 5 roles, including colonize or attack a planet), Glory To Rome (follow the leader or draw a card), and Dominion (deck building).  Luckily, it's much simpler than Race for the Galaxy, because most of the action cards in the deck are the same, until later when you research better cards.  There are lots of planets, but they don't differ that much from each other, unlike planets in Race.

One neat improvement over Dominion is that you can choose to keep a card in hand at the end of your turn rather than being forced to discard all unused cards.  This does make the game take a little longer, I think, but it removes some of the randomness and brings in some strategy.  I also like that this is not as vicious as some other games -- no attacking of others, as far as I can tell, and the only common resources that we're really fighting over are research cards, and perhaps planet cards.

There is lots of other strategy required, of course!  After one play, I could see that, but I don't have a good idea of how to put it all together.  Clearly, getting lots of military cards is one strategy.  Colonizing and then using produce and trade is another strategy.  Getting research cards (especially 7-beaker ones that are worth 5 points each) is another.  Combinations of these can definitely work.  And getting planets that further your strategy, or give you diversity (perhaps allowing you to follow a leader without spending a card) is a good helper.

Definitely want to play this more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Fresh take on proven mechanics found in other games that results in something new and its own. Gameplay is based on simple, subtle, but powerful decisions. Have found strategy elusive. Suspect that rating will increase with more play and a better grasp of how to steer the thing.

RATING: N/A
20
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
An interesting approach to deck building that combines elements of Dominion, San Juan, Glory to Rome, and Race for the Galaxy. There are five "common" cards that define actions and roles like GtR. You play a card from your hand to specify an action that you alone execute, which may be to draw more cards, get more fighters/attack a planet, colonize a planet, trade a resource, or trash cards (like the Chapel in Dominion). Then you select a card from one of the stacks of the five common cards to define a role, which allows you do more stuff, like survey new planets, conquer new planets, complete colonization of new planets, generate resources/trade on those planets (similar to craftsman/trading in San Juan), or conduct research which allows selection of more powerful and flexible cards (sort of like buying kingdom cards in Dominion, but there is no money in ED--you have to have the correct type of planets and X number of research cards). Like GtR/RftG, others can follow suit (like in GtR, but at a disadvantage) or draw one card ("Thinking" in GtR). Eventually, decks become specialized towards conquering or colonizing planets, which have VP values. But through research, the character of the decks could get substantially altered without much penalty.

Although it is not as sophisticated as Dominion in card play, there is plenty to like here, but admittedly it will take several plays for me to "get it". I haven't played Ascension or Thunderstone, but I hear this is a touch meatier than those two. There is no player interaction, except for maybe the following the role of the leader. Decks can be tailored for specific strategies, and the planets (one of the sources of VP) do not clog up the deck like in Dominion! The game conveys a similar feeling to RftG, but in a much smoother, straight-forward fashion that plays more quickly. Plus, there are less confusing icons and symbology to comprehend. My initial rating is an '8'. I'd up for another play very soon.

Update (12/29/11) 4+ plays: It's a pretty enjoyable game, but not enough for me to want to get my own copy. The game seems to end 15-20 minutes too early, and leaves me dissatisfied, kind of like Chicago Express. It's more than a filler, but ends too soon. Some of my friends think it is "just right". I'd like several more turns to fully develop my planets.

Update (12/30/11) 5+ plays: Playing twice in one night is too much; two different strategies were tried, but the game ended before either panned out. Again, the game leaves me dissatisfied. Getting dissatisfied twice in one evening is too much. Down to a '7'.

Update (05/18/12) 7 plays: I finally "get it", by managing to win twice with getting 3x advanced planets and getting one or two 7x research permanents. Understanding the tech cards seems to be the key in doing well. I'm still wary to get my own copy, but will happily suggest playing it. Still a '7' for now.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A solid deck-builder in a sea of deck builders. Sadly "solid" deck builders are a dime a dozen nowadays with terrible ones showing up even more. The game is different though than most because you're trying to build your empire with clever play of 5 main cards: Survey, Warfare, Colonize, Produce/Trade, & Research. Honestly EmDo is more on par with Race for the Galaxy, but with a deck building mechanic.

All players start with similar hands(unless playing with the expansion, more details below) and add their decks not by buying cards but when you play a card, you grab another card of the same action to not only add to your deck, but to add to your action. Also what I like is that your opponents can play the same action and benefit, albeit not as strong as the person who leads, or dissent, which allows them to draw. This eliminates solitaire turns which Dominion suffers from.

The main beef I had with the main game is that it wears thin, after only a few plays, since there isn't enough different cards to research or variety in planets. This of course is made better with the escalation expansion, which brings the game up to an 8.5. One thing I hate though is MANDATORY expansions that must be purchased to enjoy a game. Not because the expansion is great and the base game is great(think Legendary and Dark City) but when it's needed to make the game what it should have been from the get-go. The expansion makes the game that much better and varied and allows for many different paths to victory which the base game severely suffered from. 

If you own EmDo, or plan to buy it, purchase it with the expansion, without question. It turns Eminent Domain from a solid game, to a great game. Exotica is also another expansion I'd like to try, but I do not recommend that one as a day one purchase, like Escalation. You're hurting yourself not playing with the expansion

RATING: 7
It's the deckbuilding of Dominion meets the role selection and tableau building of Race For The Galaxy - an odd combination for me because I love the former and dislike the latter.

I do enjoy this game as it fixes some of the issues with Race (namely the learning curve and that leaching off other players' actions is nowhere near as critical), but it does carry over some of its problems (particularly in how knowing the cards that can be researched can be a particular advantage).

Overall, it's a fun mash-up of two types of games that I'm happy to play.

RATING: 7
2013-11-07

Initial Rating: 6.0 (November 2013)

I had huge expectations for the game, after playing it, the verdict is, it is a good game. I love the theme but I didn’t feel it when I played the game. It’s like a mix between Dominion and Glory to Rome with some RFTG added for good measure. The game art is ok and the game components are of good quality. The gameplay offers some interesting decisions (I follow?, I keep some cards at the end, which role do I play?), in particular I like the role mechanic a la Glory to Rome.

I think this is best played with 2 player or 3 fast players, more than that and I begin to feel the hated downtime. I don’t understand the name of the game very well, perhaps because English is not my first language?

I want to play it again, but for now I won’t buy it. RFTG, Dominion and GtR are superior games IMO,  I don’t think this has a lot of replayability because it seems there are too few cards making each game too similar. I have to play more to see if this is true.

Current Rating: 6.0 
[hr]
2017-07-28

It´s been years since I last played Eminent Domain. I must say that I had much more fun this time than the first one. Now, I appreciate its design a bit more. 

The decisions need to play the game aren’t very complex, but they aren’t very obvious either. When you play the game, you need to specialize and the more you do a role the better you become at it. 

Eminent Domain plays very fast, in fact the when the game ends you wish you had a little more rounds to develop your strategy. 

The luck factor isn’t very high, it is only present with the planets and the more you explore, the more you can control it.

I still prefer to play RFTG or Glory to Rome over it, but I enjoy it.
 
Bottom line, I enjoy playing Eminent Domain, for now it is a borderline 7.0 to me. I already bought all its expansions and want to try them soon.

RATING: 7
Wasn't sure I'd like this one.  It's got deckbuilding so I certainly had my doubts, but it turns out Eminent Domain scratches an itch I didn't know I had.  It's also the first real sci-fi game in my collection.  That was long over-due.

Tasty Minstrel Games is 3 for 3 with me.  This would be a 10 if the player interaction was somehow upped just a little.

Also, this is one of those games that always sparks a post game strategy discussion and we always come up with different things to try for the next game.

UPDATE: November 2012:  the solitude of this game is starting to bug me more.  Not as enthusiastic about it as I once was.  I find the decks just don't build that differently.  If I had one person that I played this with often I think I could really enjoy that, but as it is it only comes off the shelf every so often and being only kinda familiar with it from game to game causes a lot of unremarkable sessions.

RATING: 6
Eminent Domain does itself a disservice by blatantly copying the core ideas from Race for the Galaxy, Glory to Rome and Dominion. I was constantly asking myself why I wasn't playing RFTG instead which is both quicker and deeper. Dominion, not so much as I'm no fan of deckbuilders.
Considered on its own merits, Eminent Domain works and provides some strategic challenge of building up your deck. But as there's no variation in startup, I doubt its longevity. A few generic strategies will prove strong, produce/trade is a prime contender for being inferior. Interplay variety stems from the player interactions with leeching on the actions of other players being a prominent feature.

RATING: 9
I often tell people this game feels like Race for the Galaxy lite... and then am told they absolutely do NOT agree. However, you are basically doing both a little deck building and a little tablou building in a role-selection game. That is very similar to Race at its core. 

I obviously love both Race and Eminent Domain... but might recommend this game over Race because it is easier to teach and play. Race/Roll for the Galaxy are amazing games, but they look scary and Race doesn't have great table presence. This game will be a lot easier sell to people who have never played either. Move "up" to Race/Roll later - although with expansion content available this game definitely has legs to last a long time.

RATING: 7
I owned this, then traded it, and have since played with some more experienced players and found it a bit more interesting and worth exploring further. Oops. I really like that the engine has "inertia" and part of the game is managing that. On the other hand, there are rarely large score differentials at the end (first and last are usually within 3-4 points of each other) and I think the pacing is a little off; it's a bit too easy for groupthink, or just chance in planet draws, to end the game early by causing a blitz on a particular pile (or two). These two combine to make it the game winner frustratingly arbitrary sometimes.

I suspect playing the game with a public scoring track might improve this aspect significantly by making it clear to some players that they really shouldn't be helping the game to end; it's hard to keep track when scores are so close, so it's hard to tell what your incentives are. But certainly interested to play it a bit more, as long as I can find someone who still has it!

RATING: 9
I enjoy deckbuilding games. As I've mentioned, my favorite is Legendary, but this game has just enough difference to be a staple in my collection. ]

This game is different in the sense that it isn't cooperative and is seemingly more simplistic. However, there is a lot of strategy to this game, especially with the follow and dissent aspects of the game. If a player takes a role of colonization, everyone else can 'follow' and colonize as well, which opens up advantages for the next turn. OR they can dissent and draw an extra card for their next turn, which is also a great advantage.

The only complaint I have for this game is that once you play it three or so times in a sitting it gets a little stale. This is because the cards and strategies may be the same for each game, whereas in a game like Legendary the objective, characters, and villians change. My solution is to put it down and play it again a month or so later.

RATING: 7

Comments after a few plays. EmDo by itself is good, accessible and offers interesting choices. I've played many games of Race for the Galaxy, and many more games of Roll for the Galaxy, but EmDo offers me a feeling of control that is lacking from both those games. The iconography is also much, much more accessible. I've played the base game with my wife and daughter as a family game, and that worked well. I've also played with the Escalation expansion with my gaming friends, and it was a hit there, also. It is surprisingly "thinky" for a deck-builder.

There is a lot to love about EmDo, and I've backed the Exotica expansion, as well. I am concerned that some of the Escalation technologies and scenarios are overpowered, or at least unbalanced. However, even if we continue to find that this is the case, I would rather leave them in the box and keep playing this excellent game.

This game is simply great.  After 28 plays I upgraded my rating from a 9 to a 10.  Each time I play I just like it more and more.

[b]Short version[/b]: Absolutely love this game.  Easily my favorite card game.

[b]Long Version[/b]: read my review of EmDo here for my thoughts on what makes it tick.

[thread=700204][/thread]

Update: I'm finally able to put into words one of the things I really like about this design.  The point scoring is largely incremental, meaning you will generally gain a few points at a time.  There will not often be huge turns where you do so much that you vault out to a huge lead.  This game rewards consistent efficiency rather than a couple big moves and that's a focus I really appreciate, especially in a deck building format.

RATING: 7
Picked up at GenCon. We got in one play, and I'll play more to see how it goes. The leader-follow system does a lot to break the solitaire-feel of Dominion, since at any time you're hoping your opponents will take the action you need and save you some time.

It looks like there are a lot of different strategies, but since all cards are in play every game, is a master strategy possible? It's not apparent after one game at least. Unlike Dominion, small-scale choices like when to play a card are more important than large-scale ones (such as what to buy).

I think more variants and expansions will make this a solid and strategic game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
My first deck building game, pick this over Dominion because of the theme and cool mechanics. The game is neatly designed, though you must have the Bonus Planets expansion to enjoy it fully. I still feel Warfare is a bit imbalanced, but not so much that it breaks the game. Looking forward to Escalation expansion.

---

Update 20/9/2014: More plays in with 3+ players and a few problems arose. It's somewhat hard to keep track of what everyone's doing, though maybe it's not the game's fault and more likely my inability to follow the actions of other players. Also I found that for a deck building game, the mechanic that lets you keep a certain amount of cards before discarding/drawing is really clunky. There are also some problems that I don't like that inherently belong to deck building games, like it's really awkward to shuffle your deck when it has 5 cards or less, or when people make honest mistakes and overdraw.. Afterall, the 2-player game is elegant and interesting, but more than that is a bit chaotic to me.

Previously owned the bonus planets promo as well as Escalation expansion. Must have for the game if you like it imo.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Eminent Domain is a civilization-building game in which your civilization's abilities are based on a deck of Role cards. At the beginning of the game each player has the same deck of cards, with just two cards for each Role in it. Every turn you must choose a Role to execute (and like Glory to Rome or Puerto Rico, your opponents will get a chance to follow suit), and in doing so you will add one of those Role cards to your deck. When executing a Role, you can boost its effect by playing cards out of your hand matching the Role you have chosen. For example, the more you Research, the better you get at Researching (because you'll have more Research cards in your deck).

RATING: 6
LIKES:

* I like the concept of building your deck via picking the action and picking up the card as well as the opportunity for others to follow on a role.
* Game is fairly straightforward to learn and play although it will take a couple plays to play well.

DISLIKE:

* I have some concerns that once everyone knows what they are doing, the game may come down to who had the best luck of the draw for the planets or such.  I realize there are a couple other methods to points (tech/goods).  Time will tell.
* Uncertain if there is much variety to paths to victory.  I wonder if the the deciding point for the victor is possibly who chose the best secondary path to points?  

===================

All that being said, I know I took a while to warm up to Race for the Galaxy so we'll see if the same result happens here.

RATING: 5
We really enjoy [thing=28143][/thing] and [thing=19857][/thing], so we were interested in [thing=68425][/thing] when we first heard about it.  This was the first time we had heard about the whole Kickstarter process, but we decided to go in and help fund the game.  We hadn't really liked [thing=36218][/thing], but this sounded like a deck building game we might enjoy.

When it finally came in, we gave it a shot.  It was...anticlimactic.  The theme and many of the actions are taken from Race, while the role selection mechanism is lifted from Glory.  Unfortunately, it kept the boredom of Dominion.  I think what we don't like about deck builders, or particularly deck builders with a limited pool of commons cards, is that there's very little variety.  A pool of five cards just can't compete with dozens if not hundreds of different cards found in a common deck like Race or Glory.  Plus, the role cards in Eminent Domain just aren't that interesting to begin with.  I think the Planet and Technology cards are supposed to alleviate this, but generally they aren't that interesting either.  

The game had some interesting ideas, but it just fell flat with us.  The game just felt very anticlimactic and lukewarm.  The deck building feels slow and incremental, and maybe we just don't like playing deck building games.  I'm not sure if we would ever play this when we could just as well play Race or Glory instead...

RATING: 6
[2012-04] The rules should have made clear and explicit how the Icons on the upper left of the Tech cards are used. It would have been sooo simple to mention in the Boost rules that Tech cards can be discarded to boost the current Role. That would be %80 there.

The other wrinkle with the Tech Card Icons is that when tucked under a planet as a Colony, the other icons on the Tech Card are not used for anything in the way that icons on planets and double-sided face up Tech Card Icons in your Empire are used to Boost the current Role!

%100 there, but needs editing in the wording I provided here. Revise the rule book, this game could be a '9' rating. Makes it feel like the game should have been play tested more.

After having played a bunch of games I have other complaints. The Influence point values should *always* be in the upper right on all cards to help make them easier to find (by eye in your empire during the game and later for counting up for the win). All icons should be in the same place on all of the cards to make it easier to use planets and Tech cards face up in your empire to boost the current Role. As it is, they get lost among the other icons too easily.

Potentially very good game. Fun game, but so far it takes much longer than the 45 minutes noted on the box. The game takes waay too long, still over an hour. The random pulls of the +1 to deck size is extremely annoying - two games in a row I never even saw one, despite constant long survey actions. Frustration level too high but probably just dumb luck. If we could get this to the listed 45 minutes (close) then that might help.

[2012-11] ...and the 'board' is too tiny! I get annoyed at having to constantly neaten the card stacks. I don't feel like taking the time to create a larger board for this, but maybe some day. All of these nuisance factors keep us from playing the game often, which is a shame, as the problems are mostly in the presentation.

[2013-04-09] Oh, and the name! Another reason this doesn't get played is the uselessly misleading name. No one ever gets "space game" from this name. Cowboys and Aliens, maybe? wtf? Are we heading to the Pacific or to Alpha Centauri? This bad choice is further compounded by the unfortunate E.D. initials - what kind of dysfunction is wrong with this game?

A deck building game where your deck is built based on the roles you have taken earlier in the game.  Effectively, you get "better" at the role the more you take it.  Other players can follow the role like in Glory to Rome.  You have a number of special science cards that provide benefits throughout the game.  You build a tableau of planets that help you acquire technology or produce goods.  The game blends elements of Dominion, Glory to Rome and Race for the Galaxy.   An excellent and under-rated game.

RATING: 7
This is a deck-building hybrid game, with the role selection mechanic.  

While the role selection is important, it is not the heart of this game like Puerto Rico if you go different routes (esp in 2 player) in strategy then it can feel a little like multiplayer solitaire....or a race to the finish. 

Deck building is 90% by selecting a role, where you must take that card (and so your empire if better or more efficient at it).  The exception is tech cards you "buy".

EmDo could use more interaction or perhaps more strategic paths?  Even a more lengthy game in which you have time to develop something. The game and its end conditions are missing an "arc" or story.

UPDATE:  After 15 plays (with the latest plays 2p games), I think EmDo is a pretty good game.  Its not a game I think about alot or yearn to play all the time, but it is a fun "race" and has a place in the collection.  I don't think replayability is a huge concern, as I am not going to play this so much that it would become an issue.

Its not like discovering Dominion, Glory to Rome, or PR....but that's OK.  It doesn't have to be as fantastic as those games to have a place on my shelf.

RATING: 9
It's so difficult to describe this game as it is a mixture of many excellent games together, making it very difficult to pin-point any single mechanism as the majority.
After 4 games in a roll (which is my first time after playing boardgames for over 10 years), I could do nothing but rate this game as a 9, though I still don't know why we (total 7 or 8 new players) all love this game and many even asks me where to get this. Yes, it's partly because of the short game-time (less than 1 hour since the 2nd play onwards), but there must be some reason behind this which I'm yet to discover.
The system, at first sight, may appear too simple and leads to repeative play. Actually it's not. The much limited research cards and different plants all forces you to think differently in every single game.
Lastly, I'd like to say something about the distribution of the game. I'm one of the kickstarting supporter of the game and nothing could hurt our (fans of TMG) feelings more than distributing the games at Gencon before delivering to us. I know the supply maybe limited so that TMG cannot deliver the games to all kickstarters, but I think it's still more appropriate to send the games to the early supporters rather than trying to gather a buzz at Gencon.
Despite that (which is totally unrelated to the gameplay), this is simply the best buy of 2011. You should get this ASAP.

RATING: 7
More than the sum of its parts borrowed from more iconic games, Eminent Domain provides an intriguing puzzle of optimizing your own strengths while still being able to do useful things during the turns of the other players. There are a couple of easily missed small rules that delay the point where you’re really playing the game instead of just following instructions, but at that point, it plays pleasingly fast and has lots of interesting things to do and to avoid.

I’d like to explore it some more to find out whether it is as finely tuned and balanced as its forebears, and if this should turn out to be so, I might even go looking for a copy.

RATING: 8
