=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Yes, this is a deck building game, but not quite the way you would expect.  You're not building your deck to make combos, but instead to beef up your role selection portion of the game.  Instead, each turn you're going to look at your hand, decide if any actions seem worthwhile and determine if there's a particular role you need to call.  After that, you get to decide which (if any) unplayed cards you want to keep for your next turn.  So, on a turn, you're making several decisions.  Then, you have an added bonus of decided to follow or dissent on an opponent's turn.  Constantly involved in the game.

The first few plays didn't leave a positive impression on me.  This game can be a bit opaque, when you're first starting out.  Sure, you can see that you can settle & produce/trade for points.  How do you get beyond that level and how do you tie in the tech cards is what can be confusing. It made me feel like I was a terrible player (I probably still am!), but now the game is unfolding.  You start to see how you can leech off your opponents and which tech cards can benefit you at certain times.  Quite a bit to explore.

Once I learned to focus more on the role selection and hand building instead of the deck building, I started to enjoy ED a lot more.  It's more like Puerto Rico and Race for the Galaxy than like Dominion.  Calling it a deck builder almost sells it short and can give you the wrong impression.  Sure, you're adding cards to your deck every turn, but a lot of times those cards can be a hindrance (hello, Survey!).  On the other hand, you can research and add more powerful cards (due to their actions and symbols) to your deck, which gives it a little bit of the standard deck building feel. 

The Escalation expansion really improves the game.  Adding scenario cards (where your deck starts differently from game to game) really made this game extremely re-playable.  I don't see a problem with this game reaching 50+ plays.  Rules are easy enough to keep straight in your mind that this will be fine for sporadic play, as well. 

I have Exotica, as well.  However, there is so much gameplay in Escalation it might be a while before I can check out Exotica.  These are the perfect type of expansions for me.  Adds a ton of replayability without adding much complexity or changing the base game a ton.  So much to explore and try out!

I'd love a digital version! 

I don't see this surpassing Race for the Galaxy for me since it lacks the depth of play compared to it.  However, this is still an enjoyable game to explore the game play possibilities in.

Update after 49 plays:  This game is addictive.  I tend to play this in bursts - get a bunch of games in over a short period time, think about it between those plays and how I could do better, move on to something else for a little while, then it pulls me back in.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
With expansion cards
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Provisional 7 rating after 4 plays.  This seems to be an interesting game with a somewhat steep learning curve (see: Race for the Galaxy).  Although it is fairly easy to grasp the basic actions and roles, wrapping one's mind around the way the roles are executed, how the game pacing is (somewhat) controlled by the role selections, and how the technology cards contribute.  

One thing that seems to make this game substantively different from the more typical deck building games is that there are more meaningful decisions to be made.  Someone once made the comment that there are few decisions to be made in Dominion other that what to buy on a given turn. Once you have the cards in your deck, most actions can be executed by rote. In ED, the interplay between the role, the other players, and your deck/tableau both provide more options and make the decisions more meaningful.  

Not to mention the decisions involved with what to discard at the end of a turn.  Should I hold on to my Colonize cards in the hopes that one of my opponents will pick that as a role, or should I go for broke and try to research something big next turn... if I can draw one more Research card... 

Currently I think I prefer two player, although I may also like the three player extended variant as much, I just haven't tried it yet.  

After 27 games, many with the bonus planets, no 3 player extended, and many of those 2-player I have reduced my rating from 7 to 6 because I am beginning to feel that the randomness of the surveyed planets greatly contributes to a player's success or failure.  Getting lucky with planet types and/or symbols early seems to be a dominating factor in the game.  

32 plays - this game is fading quickly as I find myself often in situations where I cannot find any chain of decisions I could have made differently that would have changed the course of the game.  

36 plays - trading this away.  I really wanted to like it, but it is just not enjoyable for me.

10+ plays with 2, 3 and 4 players

RATING: 7.75
This is a different deck-builder in that you take cards by taking actions. That, along with the technology/research cards, is what made it a keeper for us.

I like the game, but I do have a couple of complaints with it. The first is that, at least in the base game, it doesn't feel like there's really a reason for having different types of goods. They're all used in exactly the same way. The second is that colonize vs. military don't feel as individual as I expected. The third is the stacks of special cards that you can get through research. I love that element of the game, but all the cards come out in every game. That reduces the variability. I wish a different selection came out each game, so that there were fewer options and greater variety between games.

(Also own Eminent Domain: Bonus Planets, Eminent Domain: Escalation, and Eminent Domain: Exotica.)

RATING: 7
Eminent Domain is a card drafting game of space exploration and domination.  It uses the same mechanic as San Juan, and Race For The Galaxy, in that when the active player uses an ability all the other players can use a less powerful variation of that ability.  This keeps everyone involved in play even when it isn't their turn.

As per all deck builders players begin the game with a starting deck of cards.  Also, players have a starting planet they can either colonize or take by force.  From there players choose an action, by taking an action card from 5 available piles, then use these cards to explore the galaxy to find more planets to exploit, gain new technologies, and build up their war machines.  Once two piles of action cards are exhausted the game ends, and players count up their points.  Whomever has the most points wins.

I like Eminent Domain quite a lot.  I prefer it to both San Juan, and Race For The Galaxy.  It has a more interesting theme than the former, and it's symbology is much more intuitive than the latter.  Play this game now!

RATING: 6
01/28/12 Played a three-player game. 
I did like Eminent Domain better than Race for the Galaxy and Core Worlds. Yet not as much as I'd hoped. I was certainly glad I tried it before buying it. For I now have no plans to purchase it. But I am willing to play it again. Points in its favor: theme, good use of icons, and the Puerto Rico style concept of role selection and the ability to glom on to another's role. Things I felt held it down: like RftG and CW no direct player interaction, deck building, the generic/interchangeable concept of the goods (the game doesn't even give them names!), and it seems to go on a bit too long. Our game only took 1.6 hours yet seemed longer. The game isn't dull, but it isn't exciting either.

RATING: 7.5
This didn't grab me initially, but the more I play it the more I really like it.  Deck-building and role selection are two of my favorite mechanisms and this game marries them very well. Plus, this can obviously be tweaked by using variants for the end-game trigger.

RATING: 6.8
A quick fun deck-building card game.  Pick a strategy -- produce/consume, military/conquer, explore/settle. (It's not as clean-cut as that -- everyone needs to explore worlds to settle or conquer to get rolling, for example.) Similar to _The Practice Effect_, you get better at things the more you do them, and have more of a chance to hold the correct cards to follow along when other people use the same action (some flavor of Glory to Rome here, but without the outrageous combos.)

RATING: 7
Met up with the illustrious designer at the FLGS Amazing Discoveries in Tucson, AZ for a demo of this game and was incredibly pleased by the experience. This game sheds a lot of the stuff I dislike about RFTG and Dominion while keeping the appealing core of both games. Furthermore, I feel like I have far more control over my success and failure on a tactical level from turn to turn, and unlike RFTG, which I really can play inside a non-interactive bubble and still win, I found I had to pay really really close attention to my opponent's strategies and plan accordingly.

This is a must buy for me and will likely replace both RFTG and Dominion in my collection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
If you haven't read any of the early reports, the game is essentially a hybrid of mechanisms from Glory to Rome, Dominion, and Race for the Galaxy. Essentially, Tasty Minstrel has taken the action cards from Race and duplicated them many times over. This set of cards forms your starting deck for a Dominion-style deck-builder, so that when you draw your initial hand you'll see, instead of Coppers and Estates, things like Develops, Settles, Produces [the names are only vaguely changed in Eminent Domain, but the functions are almost entirely a direct port from Race]. From this hand of cards, you'll be able to carry out game actions [exploring for new worlds, colonizing new worlds, producing goods, etc]. As in Glory To Rome, you can boost the strength of the action if you have matching cards, except they come from this Dominion-hand rather than your GTR-clientele. As in Glory To Rome, other players can "dissent" [read: "think"] to draw new cards or follow along to carry out the same action, again taking cards from their hand rather than a clientele of previously-played cards. After you're done, you "clean up", except unlike Dominion you can choose to keep a few cards until your next draw. The game ends when the VP tokens are gone [a la Race] or a number of role stacks are gone [a la Dominion].

The things you're doing in the game are colonizing planets [which give you points and the potential to produce and consume goods] and developing technology [which instead of going into your tableau go into your hand action-card-style as in Dominion]. You can "Survey" which lets you draw planets from the separate planet deck. Then you can engage in "Warfare" or "Colonization" which let you gain the benefits of the planets you've Surveyed [essentially, these two roles are just two ways of "paying for" your planets, akin to the two ways in Race]. Once you've got some planets, you can "Produce" or "Trade", which work exactly as they do in Race except each good is always worth 1 point and you're limited to producing/consuming a number of goods equal to the number of phase power cards you have in your hand. Finally, you can "Research" which lets you pick up the special action cards ["technologies"]; you're limited by the number of research phase cards you hold and the number and type of planets you've settled. These cards go into your hand and then get shuffled through your deck eventually, so that you can re-use them every time they come up. They do all the fancy stuff you might expect: "Settle a planet then settle again", "Produce 2 resources for 1 card", "Gain x points for each y", etc. Your standard powers.

The most original thing here is, I guess, the way the role cards are handled: When you "lead" a role on your turn, you take a card from the role stacks rather than from your hand, with the option of boosting the strength from the cards already in your hand. The little tagline the publisher's been using is that "As you take actions more often, you become better at them" because the role cards you've taken go into your deck to eventually pile up in your hand and give you even bigger boosts next time you take the role. This is cute, but it's not quite the revolution they've made it out to be. After all, the more you buy money cards in Dominion the better you get at buying money cards in the future; the more you buy cards that support a deck-thinning strategy the better you get at deck-thinning. Essentially, taking the free role card for "leading" in Eminent Domain is just a zero-cost mandatory buy. So, you'll be picking up cards that support your over-arching strategy ["Do I want to settle a bunch of planets or go for technology?" with the answer being "Take a bunch of colonize cards" or "Take a bunch of research cards", respectively] and then shuffling through your deck doing those actions. One of the bigger problems seems to be that some of the roles [Colonize] are just way too useful so everyone takes them, removing the specialization aspect and forcing everyone down similar paths.

The technology cards are cute, but are rather limited in scope [there are very few duplicated cards, so they're more analogous to GTR or Puerto Rico buildings in this respect than to Dominion action card stacks] and effect [by the time you've got enough "Research" cards to buy the "big ones", you'll probably only get to use them once or twice each]. Other aspects of the game are limited, as well; you'll probably only end up settling 5 or 6 planets, at most, during the game. Most of your action / progress will be taking new role cards into your deck, but [again] the variety here is limited by there only being 5 roles to choose from. The game ends up not having the heavy super-combo-building aspect of Race or GTR because of the limited amount and expected use of powers, not having the deck-tweaking / diversity-of-approach of Dominion because of the limited set of roles / actions and the need to have Colonize and at least a few Surveys before you can make any use of the Produce/Trade or Research powers that might let you specialize / distinguish yourself a bit. There are certainly some different strategies to pursue, but I have a feeling after you've done the two or three big strategies [Research, Colonize->Consume, maybe Survey-Colony spamming] in the game you'll have "seen it all". This would be fine if it had the depth / scope of Puerto Rico [which has only a few strategies], but it doesn't.

The main problem with the game isn't its scope, though [after all, I like Magnate quite a bit, which has a very limited scope / arc], but the complete lack of anything novel aside from the cute combination of mechanisms from the three heavy-hitters in the genre. Magnate has the funky probability distributions to deal with that drive easy-build-low-yield vs tough-build-high-yield decisions [a subset perhaps of "short term gain" vs "long term gain" tradeoffs, some of my favorite decisions / problems in all of gaming], which aren't really present in Battle Line, Lost Cities, Balloon Cup, the Catan Card Game, or anything else that it pretty blatantly rips from; this gives Magnate an entirely new problem to solve, and gives players a reason to choose it over similar titles. Eminent Domain doesn't really seem to present any new problems; it's a game entirely based on mechanics rather than meta-mechanics [the things you're using mechanics to do: optimize marginal gains, seek out game-winning combos, maintain competitive growth rates, etc]. Or maybe it's just that the central meta-mechanic ["select a specialized path of actions and pursue it more faithfully than your opponents pursue theirs"; see Puerto Rico, Navegador, etc] doesn't interest me much. It felt like Eminent Domain embodied the "nothing new under the sun" dilemma. "This does this, but that does that; pick one and do it more times than your opponent" isn't very interesting or innovative.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Update:  After 4th play, I do like this game.  But I like Core Worlds more.  I only need 1 sci-fi card game and Core Worlds is it for me.

-----

Theme is as good as Race for the Galaxy, with some tough and interesting decisions.  But way easier to learn and play than RftG.  Now playing well is another thing.

After 3 plays, I do like this game. However, it does seem like there is a lack of variety in the cards.  Especially when compared to RftG and Dominion.  I'm also learning that I'm not a fan of constantly shuffling my cards, more so if they are sleeved.

Bottom line is, this is a good solid game.  Trying to figure out if I should purchase.  I already have San Juan, which has some similarites in mechanics, and which I like quite a bit.  So I don't know if I should pick this title up or not.

I will probably wait until I give Core Worlds a try.  Yet another deck building game with a sci-fi theme, but a more war oriented theme.

RATING: 4
Played this once and thought it was fine. Trying to play it again though has been like pulling teeth. ED just feels wholly uninspired. I can definitely see the parallels to games like Race for the Galaxy and Puerto Rico, but it brings nothing new to the table. You make your deck out of a series of actions that make it feel really bland as far as deck builders go, and the special actions available are always the same from game to game, so there's no reason to change your strategy once you find something that works. The one positive of this game is that it can serve as a good stepping stone towards RftG, but given the choice, I'd play Race over this any day. Not to mention most deck builders out there.

Replaced by Race for the Galaxy, Core Worlds, etc.

RATING: 7.6
A pretty well-thought game. The mechanics are extremely easy to understand, yet the game play challenges your thinking at multiple angles as the relationship between following, leading the roles and how you get to yur goals are actually quite antigonistic. The mechanisms can probably stand the test of time, but the small downside is replayability doesn't seem very high as there are only a handful of cards to choose from, and once players understand the game (which I am sure should take a while), the game could possibly be solved. Still, it is interesting to explore the first few games with its puzzle-like mechanisms.

A game that takes the best parts of Dominion and adds a Puerto Rico style role selection mechanism. I found it quite fun on a first play, and it's pretty quick too. 
------
On a second play, I still really like this game! It just works. And there's a good tempo going from turn to turn. There is much less of a luck of the draw factor here than in other card games because you are in charge of managing your own deck, and you start with a very small deck. Similar to Dominion in that respect, but in its essentialized set of actions and tactics, ED surpasses that game. (Not too hard to do, since I rated Dominion a 3)

RATING: 4
BGG Hype-Train of 2011 (now with Kickstarter!).  Here we go again.

This game means well, and had it not come in the wake of Race for the Galaxy it might have meant more.  But it did.  Tying a deck-building theme to Race doesn't make the game better.  It makes it trendier.  It's not awful, by any means.  It's also not mind-blowing.  It's just kinda... there.  And when I'm playing a game and saying to myself, "I could be playing Race", something's off.

The biggest problem with Eminent Domain is its Act III problem, and its Act III problem is there's no Act III.  The story ends in Act II, often in mid-sentence, and as a result the ending feels rougher than it should.

ADDENDUM 1 (April 2013): OF COURSE they're making an expansion that puts to use the varied ships from the original.  OF COURSE THEY ARE.  OF COURSE that expansion could not have been included in the main game.  That might have gave the game depth.  Grrr.

RATING: 5.5
No original mechanics, it's an hybrid of Glory to Rome, Dominion and Race For The Galaxy. 

It's fun to play for those who like to build things, but there's not a lot of space for diferenciation.

Key features:
-> Fast pace
-> Constantly involved: you take decisions on other's turns
-> Easy rules, fast to teach

Contras:
-> Shallow; not a lot of posible strategies.

My fears:
-> Not enough turns to evolve decks. The game ends just when you start to do the interesting things.
-> Players with similar strategies will benefit from each other, while players with unique strategies will struggle.
-> Lack of replayability.

I've only played a couple of games, but for whatever reason, it has not ringed my bell. It has left me untouched. I tried to follow very different strategies, but the result was more or less the same.

********************
I played a 2 player game. This time, we had enough time to customize the decks, but it was the planets we got who chose the strategies, not us.

Also, I didn't like is that in midgame, you cannot adapt your strategy. It's not possible to get rid of that many cards and switching, for example, to a production/trade tactic would be extremely inneffective.

Science is the major source of points. Once you get it going, after 2-3 science roles, it's 2-5 points per ROLE. That's way too much, much more effective than any other strategy.

RATING: 6
Deck-building game where you do an action (for yourself) and select a role (that others can use in a bit San Juan style) every round. The role card then goes into your deck. I simply didn't like this "role goes to deck" - mechanic which also is triggering ending in game. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A deckbuilder that rewards concentrated play; ie, focus in a given area. Like the structure of its game turn, as it keeps the other players involved: 1) perform an action that only you benefit from; 2) perform a role (action) that you get a bonus for, but others can also benefit either by playing the same action or by taking a card into their hand; 3) rebuild your hand up to 5, keeping all, some or none of the cards left in hand. It is the last part of the turn -- rebuilding your hand -- that makes this game shine, as you want to have cards in your hand that you can use on another player's turn.

The theme works, but it is plastered on wafer-thin. This does not work to its detriment, though, and some who might not did the whole "space" thing (like my GF) might just get into this one.

Can't wait to play it again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Kickstart finally came through!

Deck building and role selection with a space colonisation theme. [i]Eminent Domain[/i] feels similar to [i]Glory to Rome[/i], in that after each player action the others have the option of joining-in or drawing a card; and perhaps it suffers from that comparison, because [i]GtR[/i] is a brilliant game.
[i]Eminent Domain[/i] has been a long time coming, and the publishers have used the time to provide fantastic illustrations and polish the rules.
The game looks fantastic, but [i]Eminent Domain[/i] feels sightly lacking. There are not enough interesting combinations to explore, and compared with GtR there is too little interaction. EmDo is good, but not great.
The three player game seems best: four players and the game seems to end a little too soon, at the point when players are beginning to exploit their "engine". Two player games seem to drag on longer than necessary. Maybe a little more playtesting was required. [i]Eminent Domain[/i] seems very likely to get at least one expansion, and I hope it does. In the meantime it is a pretty good game that leaves me wanting a little more.

This has become one of my favorite engine builders, and favorite games in general.  I really like how the deck building works, and this game captures the exploration element of games like Race for the Galaxy. I think this game is easy enough to grasp for a new player and has enough depth to bring players back.

I enjoy both expansions a ton. I think the tech is very thematic and really well done. I hesitate to play them with new players but I bring them to the table every chance that I get.

This game is really a game about exploration and possibilities. Every time I play it there is an excitement about putting together the engine and trying to react to what other players do. For a game that I think has alot of control there is also a ton of variety, which is a great combination in my opinion.

RATING: 8
Playtested my own prototype. About 20 games, mostly 2 players. You may like the mechanics or not but the game is solid as stone. I tried to crash it and it won't. At first you'll try winning going full colonize and you may succeed for a while, but then... you'll try going full warfare and think you got it, but then... I think I'll try going full research but that takes knowing the tech cards very well (and those changed in the playtest version, so). From my experience, radical strategies work better in a 2 player setup. But with 4 people, a more balanced approach pays off much better. I think this is due to the "follow" feature, a neat design addition to this game.

RATING: 7
規則:
*圖板放中間，各種腳色卡分類放在各疊，科技卡依星球需求分成三疊，影響力除了特殊的8顆先放旁邊其他放圖板上
*每個玩家拿1政治1戰爭，其他各2張共10張組成牌庫，並拿一張隨機起始星球蓋著放前面，幫助卡隨便發有起始標記的起始，每人抽五張牌開始
*遊戲根據人數結束條件不同，達成條件且每人回合相同遊戲結束，每人每次分成三個階段
1.出一張牌執行行動(可跳過)
2.從中央選擇一張腳色卡做該動作，再從手牌出一樣的加強(星球卡有符合也會加強)，並獲得leader獎勵，其他人順時鐘選擇抽一張牌或是跟隨(出一樣的牌做腳色動作)
3.可棄任意數量手牌，手牌再補到上限(起始5)
*結束條件為影響力標記拿完或是2/3/4人時1/1/2種腳色抽完，3人變體(每種移除兩張，戰爭一張)為2種腳色抽完，結算所有星球卡跟科技卡還有影響力標記總和，最高獲勝(最後回合不夠拿就拿一開始移掉的8個)
**卡片介紹
>政治卡 1:移除該牌並從中央牌庫拿一張牌到手上
>探索 1:抽兩張 2:看星球牌堆(探索符號減1張)，挑一張蓋著放面前，其他開著棄掉，沒得抽再把棄牌洗勻 Bonus:多看一張
>戰爭 1:拿一架飛機或是支付X架(星球卡背面左邊)將星球翻面 2:每個符號拿一架 Bonus:改為支付飛機翻面星球
>殖民 1:將該卡塞到星球卡下或是翻面該星球如果殖民張數大於星球卡右邊需求 2:每張都塞到任意星球下 Bonus:可改作翻面動作
*星球卡正面符合可減面殖民需求，翻面卡卡片都到棄牌區
>生產或交易: 1:一個星球生產一個對應資源或是賣掉一個資源換一分 2:每個符號放一個資源到星球上(資源格要空著)或是每個符號賣掉一個資源換一分 Bonus:只有在中央牌庫空才有，額外多獲得一個符號
>研發 1:移除兩張卡(可包括行動這張) 2:符合星球跟符號個數拿對應科技卡(，可自由選，不夠就不拿變成選角色卡不動作) Bonus:中央用完就多一符號
*有六張沒有行動的是放前面，且兩面只能選一面使用
****BGG SOLO 變體*****
*NPC玩家手牌3政治3生產其他各2，NPC面前有三區 探索區 殖民區 分數區
*你永遠起始，兩人各一個起始星球
*自己行動的腳色階段(2)，NPC永遠跟隨
*NPC階段
1.開兩張牌，沒牌就將棄牌洗勻
2.NPC選擇兩張中對應中央圖板最左邊的行動做(政治卡視為最右邊)，除非該行動沒好處就選另一張，如果兩個行動都沒用就選政治行動，移除中央牌推該行動卡一張，之後全部棄掉，行動如果不能做bonus就做原本的
>探索 拿一張卡蓋著放NPC探索區 Bonus拿兩張選擇分數高的放探索區
>戰爭 拿一架飛機 Bonus 有飛機的話就還一架飛機把星球卡翻面放到分數區(順位 探索區戰鬥值最低 > 分數高 > 有資源 > 殖民區)
>殖民 將探索區殖民值最低的移到殖民區 Bonus 移動殖民區一個到分數區 順位同上
>生產/交易 有貨就賣(全部都賣掉換1分)不然就產貨(每個分數區都產一個) 跟隨時要跟你一樣
>研發 星球牌推一直開到有三種基本星之一，計算分數區該星數量，隨機抽對應星球數的卡牌，沒的抽降一格抽，有分數的放分數區，沒有的棄掉 Bonus 獲得一個藍色影響力分數，並且此回合星球數+1
>政治 拿2分

RATING: 7
Your VP collection aim is mainly achieved by building planets and then using those planets to trade in goods. There's a Survey action you can choose to draw planet cards and place 1 into your tableau, but you then need to bring the planet alive by using later actions to get the needed number of colonise cards (Colonize action) or warships (Warship action). Once they're alive, you use the Harvest action to get goods, and then Harvest again to trade them for VPs. To allow differentiation of strategy, a 5th action is available to buy Tech cards which are useful for their actions and bonuses. That's the essence. In play itself, first you get to play a free Action from your hand of cards which no one else can leach from. Then you choose one of the main 5 actions. You draw the appropriate action card and add it to your personal deck (from which your hand is drawn), and then you get to soup it up with cards from your hand (eg draw survey, play 2 more surveys from your hand and you get to choose from 3 planets, and you've added another survey to your personal deck for future use as well). The strategy thoughts then are whether to concentrate on just a few action types so you'll always be able to soup them up, or do you spread out your deck across all the actions so that you can take advantage of what everyone else does in their turn - because when anyone draws a card and does that action, anyone with that same action card in their hand can tag along and do it too. As a result, there's very little downtime. The game was surprisingly tricky to learn and play correctly at first, given the powers and techs have to be learnt, but it seems simple after the first play. It leaves you with an urge to play again and try different deck building strategies - tech heavy, or specialised in 2 or 3 fields, or balanced. My doubts are that in a 4 player, and even a 3 player, is that: 
a) there aren't enough turns to be able to afford actions to strip decks and work towards big techs (and have enough turns to take advantage of them), and 
b) players who have similar strategies will pull away from divergent players and end the game early
c) there might not be enough game to game variety to sustain ongoing play, compared to other card-driven games like Innovation or Glory To Rome which have oodles of different powers to experience. 

Still, it's been interesting enough to explore and I'll get my money's worth while determining how big these issues are re long term stayability.

RATING: 8
11/13/11 - Multiple roles and various paths to victory make this the best deck building card game I have played. The art and lack of art on the cards really fit into the Sci-fi theme of the game. It gets a 8 and has jumped onto my wishlist.

12/28/11 - It is now in my collection and I have managed to introduce it to several new people. So far, everyone has liked the game and I have watched their choices of roles. It seems everyone focuses on Colonize and Research cards with a smattering of Survey cards.
 
I myself have been taking the time to try several different role paths to see how they play and if they are viable choices. My first play, I thought why would anyone want to follow or play Produce/Trade. I researched it away not giving it a second thought.

Now that I have tried it a few times and it works well with Warfare or Colonize to rack up some VP. So what I am saying is this is a solid design and I feel all the roles, if played right, can lead to victory.

RATING: 6.5
Based on one play: I played my first game with the Escalation expansion and I would not suggest that. The base mechanic is similar to many other deck builders with a slight twist in how you take actions and select a role for which others can then follow. However best to play a few game without the expansion since it adds a lot of technology card that you can buy and are difficult to sort out on your first play, plus the starting setup for each player varies causing a strong learning curve for new players. I will play again but only the base game to get a real feel.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A great deck-builder, simple enough with the core cards and augmented with the researchable tech cards. The role selection takes a bit for people to get used to in m experience but once it clicks the game runs very smoothly. I would highly recommend getting hold of the expansion if you like the base game as the scenario cards are a great way of having asymmetric starting positions.
Although it is a deck-builder it is very much a race to build an engine (your deck) and run it to gain points, something I wasn’t really taking into account when I first started playing and got caught out when the game ended before I could sell a load of goods or attack/settle a planet.

The only issue I have with the game is that when you first start playing your knowledge of the researchable techs is so small you either don’t utilise them or don’t know which ones to buy. After 10 games I still feel that I need to understand that deck better as they are key to building a good deck. I imagine that this game would get even better if your group played it often enough that people knew which techs they wanted early on.

RATING: 6
This game combines the deckbuilding concept with a Glory to Rome-style role selection mechanic.  The twist is, the more you do a particular action, the more of that action card you get in your deck, which of course means that all players will immediately specialize.  Where this game falls flat for me is in the lack of variety.  All of the action cards are completely identical, and the planet cards only vary by a few icons.  Even the Research cards, which I guess add a little variety to the actions, only tended to differ by a few words.  Some may like the simplicity, but I wanted a little more spice.

(2 plays)

RATING: 9
(1 play) The thing that led me to try out this game was that it is a "deck-building" game, except that the decks are build in-game, as you play - not in a corner for hours and hours, pining over and over and over about hundreds of cards in a big collection that you have to mix and match and consider every which way (had enough of that kind of game).

The verdict - the game worked great! I really enjoyed the fast turns, the sense of building a space-faring civilization, the fast turns, the numerous difficult choices, the fast turns, and the slow tweaking of your deck of cards every turn to become the civilization you want. The only negative was the amount of time it took to really let the game "click". We had it by the end of the first game, so that's pretty great - but that first game took some patience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Having grown accustomed to Ascension where deckbuilding means giant combos, this is another Dominion style deckbuilder where you're not so much building combos as simply changing the odds of getting more cards of certain types.

Use a card to do an action, then take a card from the center tableau to do a role, powered with more cards from your hand. Other people then get the opportunity to either use that same role (with slightly reduced effectiveness) or draw a card, then you discard whatever you want and redraw to 5. Then the next player repeats. So it's all about not letting other people ride on your roles and benefit from them.

I'd say it's about as exciting as Arctic Scavengers, but I prefer the feel of that. I want to have fun when I play a deckbuilder, not just be trying to solve a puzzle to win.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Eminent Domain is basically a Frankengame, assembled from the mechanisms of three other games. Race for the Galaxy is deeper, Dominion is more streamlined, and Glory to Rome is funnier and has more eye contact. If you game a lot, maybe marginal improvements are worth seeking out. I don't, and I find "mechanism refinement" to provide diminishing returns in a hurry. 

Yes, collage and assemblage can be cool and take you strange places (see: Cornell, Joseph), but I didn't find any spark here. It was familiar, and it technically functioned. I thought the ability to keep a card in your hand before you drew a new hand was kind of neat, but overall, this was an invitation to stand at the Mechanism Buffet and serve yourself the same frozen food available at any mid-grade suburban family restaurant in a strip mall.

RATING: 9
I feel like the mechanism Eminent Domain introduced to add player interaction to deckbuilding games is the best way to do it. You choose an action to take, and everyone else does the same thing except diminished somewhat. The phrase I like to think of is "passive interaction", since while you're directly affecting what your opponents are doing, it's more a consideration than the main tactic.

People complain about the tech cards for multiple reasons all the time, but I honestly don't see what the big problem is. The learning curve isn't that high since most of the cards are just "improved" versions of the basic ones. It's uncommon to have every single tech card available to purchase, too, so your options are more realistically between 1/3 or less of the total pool.

RATING: 8.1

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Eminent Domain is deck building game in the style of Dominion (thus presumably the title). Unfortunately while it has many interesting aspects the whole is deeply disappointing. The exact problem is hard to put my finger on, but I think it is this. Eminent Domain will only take about a dozen rounds or so to play. In the first three or four you will shape your deck in such a way as to shape your strategy for the rest of the game. So far this is identical with the dynamic of Dominion. But in Dominion the cards you use to alter your deck change randomly between each game - in Eminent Dominion the cards are always the same. So there is no equivalent dynamic of seeking out best combo from a new combination of cards - you never seen anything new.
Where ED should develop its own dynamic (and I would much rather this than a Dominion clone) is that instead of completely committing to one strategy at the beginning you should be adjusting and altering your strategy over the game. But there the game falls down. It all ends a little too fast to allow a shift in strategy, and in practice the cards that shape a deck in the early part of the game simply do not have enough impact on a 20 card deck to allow this option mid-game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
An intriguing mix of mechanics from many popular card games - deck-building as pioneered by Dominion and copied by countless others, role selection with leading/following, as in Glory to Rome, with a bit of tableau building a la Race for the Galaxy thrown in (and of course the theme, and several of the roles themselves, are very reminiscent of RftG). Unfortunately, overall EmDo is a weaker game than any of those which provided its ingredients - not bad, but a little dull and definitely not as good a game as it might have been.

And having played with the Escalation expansion, I think they really should have included that with the base game. It still doesn't make EmDo as good as the games which inspired it, but it provides much that was missing from the original base game.

RATING: 7
A very solid deck builder.  The only real difficulty at present is the fact that the starting planets very much set up the likely tech strategy - metallic leads to war/survey, fertile to colonisation & advanced to research/commerce.  In 2 player, this can lead to one player going down the war route, the other down colonisation - this in turn results in a degree of multi player solitaire as neither will follow the other.  

Also, due to the limited tech tree, there are questions as to diversity of strategies.  Nevertheless, my wife and I enjoy it and are agreed that it is worth pulling out at least one a month.

It looks like Escalation will really spice up the game.

RATING: 4
i liked the idea of this game and tried selling my friends on it. one day at the gameshop i was teaching this and a guy sat down who'd seen it online. i had mostly ignored research because the tech deck is so cluttered and unmemorable compared to what i considered the smooth, clean meat of the game. this guy, however, immediately focused on research. he grabbed a few techs, and then proceeded to stomp me and the learning players. okay. we tried again with everybody understanding a bit more about research and maybe a little less going easy on the new players. that slowed things down as they each had to read through the entire damn tech deck, but worse it fed him research follows! he quickly sussed out an untapped research path and push it through towards an even less-contested victory. i should have just given him the box and everything, but, having "solved" the game, he was done. so am i. i'm done. this game is ruined for me. i thought the exploration, colonization, and manufacturing were fun, but apparently that's the side you fall back to when you've got the right research. maybe this awkward puddle of non-random tech rewards repeated plays, but i'll never know. mine is for trade/sale alongside the shiney-boxed kickstarter expansions, if you're curious. or you can go play what this was supposed to be a deckbuilder version of, Race for the Galaxy: a well-recommended game you probably already own which certainly rewards repeated plays.

RATING: 6

First play of this was somewhat disappointing - with two players, the game seemed to drag on forever. We ended up calling it after I got my warfare engine flowing faster than her colonize/settle one. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Visual Appeal: (3)
I really think the art on some of these cards could be better. It looks nice, but not stunning. The box art, however, is very nice. I like the small amount of table space that this game takes up. Less than deck building standards like Dominion, but it never feels constrained.
 
Components: (4)
The components are high quality and the designer went with a glossy finish to the centerpiece and cards rather than a matte finish. It makes the cards really pop in a nice way, but they feel like they might wear quickly so I immediately sleeved them. I am glad that the "Escalation" expansion will add a mechanic for the different sized ships. Everyone always asks, "why are they different sizes?"

Theme: (3)
This is a game where mechanisms come before theme. The actions and effects of the cards make sense: Colonize makes colonies, produce makes resources etc. However, most of the theme comes from research and for everyone with whom I've played, this is coincidentally the most intriguing part of the game. Without the research there's little point in having different planet types and there would be little to no differentiation in strategy. This is definitely the one area that makes the game fun and re-playable.

Learning: (5)
This game is a breeze to teach. By this point everyone that I know has played Dominion, and they understand the concept of hand management is more or less the same. It takes a little more time to talk about leading/following roles, but this is the only real point of potential confusion. The rulebook does a great job illustrating the flow of the game.

Gameplay: (4)
EmDom has very few ways in which you can directly affect your opponent. It is like Race for the Galaxy in this way. However, one of my favorite things to do in competitive games is predict my opponents' moves. In this game (and Race too, but in a limited way) you are rewarded for predicting correctly and you can save yourself actions later down the road by planning this way. Sometimes you have to weigh your options. Maybe it is better to rush and end the game before your opponent gets things going. This pressure is also an enjoyable metagame for me as I am often slinging taunts at the same time.

Overall Fun: (4)
This game is a thinker of a card game. I haven't fallen victim to much AP due to my play style being more of a "play your instincts" type, but I've seen a few players who just don't know what tech tree they want to do because the planet surveys are so unpredictable. It is definitely one that I want to play often and it hasn't yet worn out it's welcome.

RATING: 5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very derivative, but has a charm all of its own, and it's fun trying out different strategies game to game. Probably the most charming aspect for me is its simplicity. You have your deck and there's a satisfying flexibility to how you can build and shape it. With four players the game seems to end just as it's getting started. You don't really get a good run at using your technologies before it's all over. With two players it seems to go on a bit long if the players are running different strategies. Three players might be about right, but it seems to depend on what strategies the different players are taking - a trade strategy will struggle if the other two players are aggressively following say, a colonise / research strategy. The Colonise deck will be depleted quick as a flash and the game will be a bit of a dud. The more varied the strategies between the players the more fun the game it seems. It's a weird game in this respect.

It's a good game. Player interaction is fairly low, but more subtle than you think as players develop their empires and their various symbols grow. Strategic decisions are interesting and a good survey base is more important than I first thought it was as you fish for planets to compliment your strategy. It's fun to try something out, experiment and be different. How long that'll be fun time will tell. I don't think I'd bother with it if I was a Race for the Galaxy fan (which I'm not), but like I said, it has a charm all of its own and judged on its own there's fun to be had.  A good game takes about 40 minutes and gives you something to think about. Looking forward to exploring further.

----------------

After exploring further I've not grown tired of it at all. Quite the opposite to growing weary of it, I have seen the light and what the fanboys have been harping on about. Maybe I'm a bit slow, but it took about five games to work out I was only using half my brain. Maybe I needed that time for the rules to click so I could then focus on strategy? Building up an empire where you can effectively follow another player's role without expending any cards has become very interesting. Instead of using the extra icons to strengthen your roles, you can use them to diversify and set yourself up on your turn for a much bigger play than if you had just been picking up extra card through desisting. The game has a good length and plays well with 2-4 players. Thankfully there's more player interaction than I first thought. You're constantly looking at timing your role actions for when it won't benefit your opponents, or even changing your strategy if need be. Absolutely worth having a copy in the collection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Sort of like a cross between Glory to Rome, Race for the Galaxy, and Dominion. There is a lot of subtlety in the interactions between players, and in the timing for this game. I really enjoy researching for level 2 and 3 tech cards. They don't change between games, but there is a decent amount of variety in which cards you will choose to take due to world considerations and the strategy you've chosen.

I need to figure out the timing on switching into heavy produce/trade, as well as consider how good military vs colonize is. 

The game lasts much longer if people are not overlapping much in their role selection. 

With inexperienced players, Eminent Domain can sometimes devolve into everyone doing settle spam (there's no reason it couldn't be conquer spam, but I haven't seen it), which makes the game shorter and far more luck based than if players actually consider the best option available to them.

-----------------------

Both military and colonize are viable, and their strengths depend on which worlds you have. I sometimes try to delay my decision until I've surveyed a few times in order to make a more informed decision. Trade is viable, but switching from colonize/warfare into trade is tricky, and new players often dismiss it due to this difficulty. It's easy to try to switch too soon, in which case you won't be making many points off it at first, and you'll have an inefficient deck. It's also easy to wait too long, in which case you might not get any return from your investment before the game ends.

RATING: 1
[u]First impression:[/u]
San Juan meets deck building while trying to look like RFTG. Some clever things like adding cards to the deck being mandatory and at the same time being used as a timing mechanism. While it is much, much simpler to grasp than RFTG, maybe even San Juan - as everything is done with copies of 5 basic card and the unique cards are used sparingly - it has the same problem as Dominion for me. The logic of playing well is to wrap your head around the system which doesn't exactly hint at how does it want to be used and I guess there is space to explore there, but I don't care for learning counter-intuitive and/or oblique strategies when they're driven by the game (not the players). The game sets strict limitations and asks for optimization within those. Interaction exists and is more important than in RFTG, but it's in the end it's just same old leeching. Another thing I'm not fond of is graphic design, I generally like contemporary approaches, however it doesn't present a thematic narrative, like RFTG does. Instead it's more like being in charge of an empire, but using gameboy controls, so you don't get confused, and with this you're as far from the real going ons as the politicians are these days. 

Still the theme and  the unique playstyle make it more interesting than San Juan for me, but Fleet blows this completely out of water. For a MPS I like RFTG better and for interaction - Fleet.

[u]latest impression:[/u]
Currently still holding a bit above 1.0 rank, but it's close. It's just so uninspiring and bland design which manages to be much more meh than its influences. 
Elaboration: if memory serves the gameplay is about optimisation and memorising those few special cards that shape a "strategy" which means the playing space is very controlled and restricted and like a good little maze rat you count your steps and execute your path to grab a bit more cheese than other rats. If this sounds fun, more power to you. A few cardgames of this type lack so much excitement in gameplay and visuals as this one (for me).

[u]Rating down to 1.0 because[/u]: uninspired. Yeah it's a crime.  
[hr]The more I played this the more bland and pedestrian it felt. It's a kinda blend of tableau builders (like RFTG) and deck building, but being less than the sum of its parts. They decided to go down the path of having mostly a lot of the same cards, so it's more crucial to optimise around them, then to think on the spot how they go together (like in RFTG). Visuals are terribly uninspiring, not evoking anything and mostly looking like a game console. Oh and there's some leeching added to optimisation. Wheee!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

It's a space-themed deck-building-ish game that blends some elements of Glory to Rome with Dominion and Race for the Galaxy. There are 2 main ways to get VP: control planets (either colonize them, or attack them with armies) or produce ("harvest") goods from your worlds then trade those in for VP. Of course, you'll generally do at least a little of both. The key highlights in comparison to those other games:
- on your turn, you choose a role; everyone else may either follow (for a weaker version, usually) or draw a card
- the chosen role is taken from a common supply and simply added to your deck; no purchasing required
- you can boost the role by also playing matching cards (icons) from your hand
- you can hold some of your cards at the end of your turn

Clearly, I enjoyed my games. Thinking more about it, though, you realize that little changes from game to game: only the order in which planets happen to come up. And the planets have only minor differences: 3 types, with variable defense/colonization requirements, some with one or two of 4 possible goods (which are really not different from one another). I think this game is going to need some expansions -- maybe new planets, additional roles, some differentiation between the goods -- if it's to have any kind of staying power.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The "learning variant" of the game without the technology cards is a bit too lean for me.

At first what I saw was a Race for the Galaxy clone but once it was taught to me and I started to play I saw that while it may have gotten a lot of its influence and ideas from RftG it changed enough of the formula to have its own personality.  The idea of having the different roles you play actually be the collection of cards in your deck is original enough to be interesting and the uses of these roles seems well balanced and implemented.

Where the game fell short is the lack of variety.  Once the game was up and running it became clear that there wasn't enough to keep me interested through even the end of that first game.  There are only really 3 types of planets (ones that gave bonuses to hand size or symbols for boosting, ones that held multiple resources, and ones that gave you huge amounts of victory points) and because of that there aren't very many interesting strategies to pursue during the game.  You could adjust a little bit to take advantage of the bonus symbols or resource slots on your planets but for the most part what you do at the beginning of the game is what you do in the middle and end of the game.  Namely either focus on warfare, colonization, or a hybrid of the two.

Like I said, the game is different enough to be its own thing rather than just another Race for the Galaxy clone.  It's just cripplingly average.

The above comments only refer to the game without technology cards and with the technology cards it is elevated some.  Each player gets to diversify a little more, the research role is more useful, and each player is given more options for goals and strategy.  I always really appreciated the neat mechanic of collecting cards in your deck that also boosted/activated the different roles you could take.  It was an original mechanic that worked reasonably well and with the technology cards the game has more personality.

That said though I think I would always rather play RftG or a deck builder like Dominion over this.  While it is original in its mechanics and has more teeth when playing with the technology cards I feel like the whole thing still boils down to a "colonize or warfare" strategy which isn't the most interesting for me.  But with the technology implemented it isn't something I would balk too much at playing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Unlike what others may lead you to believe, this game is HEAVILY built on the mechanics of Glory to Rome.  Sure it has similarities to race for the Galaxy and Dominion; but those are only uncles, GtR is the granddaddy.  The only similarities with Dominion are deck building and the fact that poor shuffling results in a better hand.  With RftG, it shares planet conquering/colonizing and Harvest/trade.  But the core concept it takes from GtR.  The game plays out by a player taking an action.  That action is generally only performed by the active player, but then the player drafts a card as a role from the available role cards.  This role is played, the active player then gets in add cards of the same type to increase that action, all other players then have the option of performing follow-up actions using that same card type by playing those cards from their hand or they may pass and draw two cards.  

So in reality there is nothing innovative here.  But I don’t want to sell it short.  Put all these things together and the game is good.  It is short, eventful, engaging and fun.  

I have upped my rating to 7 after a few more plays.  It does seem to have legs.  I think it actually has distilled deck building to a little more puity.  In this more than any other deck bloat can be managed and NEEDS to be managed in order to win.

RATING: 7.5
I've always lumped this game with Core Worlds because they came out at the same time and they are both deckbuilders about conquering planets. But I was surprised at how differently they played. I really liked the idea of Core Worlds, but the game took too long and it was tough to get any combos going. Eminent Domain on the other hand felt like an actual deckbuilder. I really like how the role selection works ala Puerto Rico and that you can always dissent to draw an extra card. I love how the action you take determines which card goes in your deck. It's funny how you might want to take the same action repeatedly, but then you need to keep getting rid of those cards. I like how researching works, although the first game I thought you had to take the card that was on top of the stack. I'm a little worried about replayability because it seems like there are only two overarching strategies (military vs colonize) and those strategies appeared to be fairly straightforward. Of course I could be wrong since I only played it twice. Either way, my wife wouldn't enjoy this so I doubt I'll be able to play it enough where replayability becomes an issue.

RATING: 7
A good game that, as up to now, seems a little limited by the amount of cards it has. The choice of the main actions is limited (in the likes of Race for the Galaxy), but unlike Race for the Galaxy or Dominion, the different cards come in only one way: technology cards.

The tech cards have a few uses: as per the icons it has, per its power and earning VPs (this is only true for the higher rank cards). But they take a while to get, and considering the "normal" game of ending when 2 rows of actions are empty, it seems to not really be worth - planets and trade earn more VPs and going by tech cards take you somewaht away from this.

Still, what bothered me the most was that, when playing with people not used to to it, you have two options: playing a blander game, without the techs, or going full on and having to halt the game several times while people search and try to understand what the techs do. Not fun either way.

RATING: 6
This game has a lot of hype to live up to, for various reasons, and in a way, I think it does.  It takes the idea of a deck-building game but makes it different enough so that it requires a different thinking to bring to the game to do well.  There's no such thing as a "Big Money" strategy here, because there are multiple fronts you have to manage over the course of the game.  Planets are points-getters, but so is a strong produce/trade tactic, and then you have to ask yourself if you want to add planets to your tableau by colonizing them or conquering them.  It's a neat balance that keeps the game moving pretty swiftly, and it has a nice payout for the time invested.  I'm glad to finally have it.

1 play: 2 players

RATING: N/A
Survey the galaxy to expand your civilization... Will you colonize nearby planets, or take them over by force? Harvest resources for trade, and do research to improve your technology. Build the best civilization and win the game!
\13\\13\
Eminent Domain is a civilization building game in which your civilization's abilities are based on a deck of Role cards. At the beginning of the game each player has the same deck of cards, with just 2 cards for each Role in it. Every turn you must choose a Role to execute (and like Glory to Rome or Puerto Rico, your opponents will get a chance to follow suit), and in doing so you will add one of those Role cards to your deck. When executing a Role, you can boost its effect by playing cards out of your hand matching the Role you have chosen. So for example, the more you Research, the better you get at Researching (because you'll have more Research cards in your deck).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
2013-07-02
This was an easy one to jump into and enjoy from the very first play. I really like how the system portrays the different roles your growing empire can take. In my first play, being warlike and invading planets rather than peacefully colonizing them meant that my army would become larger and more likely to strike, while making peaceful colonization less lucrative. I think it's a clever way to make the deckbuilding system actually fit the theme. While there is no confrontation between players, each player's choice on how to spend their turn decides what benefit the others can acquire, so you are constantly keeping up with the activity.

RATING: 7
#156/500 with promo planets and "super secret surprise"

Have only played twice but it didn't blow me away as expected. I read it may take a few plays so I'm hoping to get several more in and revisit the score.

I liked how you got better at things the more you did them. I wasn't crazy about how it seems you need to do something twice to get it done (colonize -> settle, warfare to get ships then to attack) but maybe I wasn't leeching correctly. The technologies weren't as exciting as I thought they would be either. Games went kind of long with 4 too.

With a couple more plays, things are clicking better. The optional action phase, when utilized correctly, is the answer to my complaint of "doing things twice". Techs are a little more interesting now that I noticed there are common ones in the decks but they have different symbols. Bumped up the rating a little. Also quicker with 3.

Rating bumped up. Played a few more games and each one saw a new strategy, there are really a ton of layers to this game. Played very well 2-player but I would expect 3 to be the best, and even better when everyone knows the game well. Every time we play I notice a few more subtle things that really make me like the game that much more. Really well designed and looking forward to Escalation.

RATING: 8.5
Interesting as a 2 player game, sweetspot probably 3. Can be quite strategic and brainburny at times, but without too much scope for AP. Components are mostly very nice.

Most of the game's cards are abstract actions, represented by cards with no pictures, just symbols. Rather than building tangible things like space stations, you refine your deck of action cards to enable you to acquire victory points through claiming planets/technologies/trade.

Plenty of technologies and viable strategies give the game plenty of replayability.

With fewer players the game is very tense and requires a high level of concentration. As the player number increases, the game becomes more relaxed but can start to become confusing as players take actions during each other's turns.

The end of the game is very anti-climactic.

Raised from 8 to 8.5 with expansion which adds more use for spaceships and different start scenarios, amongst other cool things.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a rather interesting re-approach to deckbuilding.  I find it especially interesting that if you want to do something, you just do it, and thus "get better at it" by adding the card to your deck.

RATING: 7
For me, the drawback of Race for the Galaxy is the randomness you encounter during the game.  You can embark on what seems like a winning strategy, only to lose because of bad luck on card draws (of course, adapting to that is part of the challenge of Race).

This game has a similar feel to RftG but with far more certainty for the strategy you choose.  While there is still an element of randomness to it, you feel like your strategy vs. other players' strategy is what is making or breaking who wins.

I'd still recommend Race for the Galaxy over this one, but if you enjoy Race and are looking for something with a bit less randomness to it, this is a fun option.
